==PAGE:1==
Modeling and Mitigating Power Line
Mast Instability in Arctic Permafrost: A
Case Study from Sisimiut, Greenland
Author:
Samuel Schuler (s221588)
Supervisors:
Gustav Grimstad (NTNU)
Thomas Ingeman-Nielsen & Tom De Ville (DTU)
Nicolai Gjerde Bennedsen (Sweco Danmark A/S)
11.06.2025
==PAGE:2==
Preface
This thesis is submitted as a requirement for the completion of the degree Master of Science in
Civil Engineering at the Technical University of Denmark (DTU) and the degree Master of Science
in Cold Climate Engineering at the Norwegian University of Science and Technology (NTNU) (30
ECTS). The research was carried out from January to June 2025, building on fieldwork performed
in September 2024 in cooperation with Nukissiorfiit, the Greenlandic utility company. Initial data
processing and methodology development were undertaken as part of the specialization project
TBM4501 at NTNU in the fall semester of 2025.
Supervised by Gustav Grimstad (NTNU), Thomas Ingeman-Nielsen (DTU), and Nicolai Gjerde
Bennedsen (SWECO Danmark A/S), and in collaboration with Tom De Ville’s PhD research on
Assessing the Impact of Permafrost Thaw on Critical Infrastructure in Arctic Communities, this
research provides insights into infrastructure resilience in Arctic permafrost regions, investigating
potential causes of foundation instability and offering recommendations for improving the long-
term stability of power lines in challenging environments.
Trondheim, 11.06.2025
Samuel Schuler
i
==PAGE:3==
Abstract
This thesis investigates the geotechnical stability of power line masts in permafrost terrain near
Sisimiut, West Greenland, focusing on identifying mechanisms behind observed foundation dis-
placements. Two representative cases are analyzed in detail: Mast 7, which remains relatively
stable, and Mast 9, which has experienced significant deformation. Field investigations included
GNSS displacement measurements, borehole drilling, temperature profiling, and Electrical Resistiv-
ity Tomography. Laboratory tests on undisturbed frozen samples provided thermal and mechanical
soil parameters, including data from temperature-controlled Oedometer tests. The soil profile at
Mast 9 is characterized by finer-grained material, higher water content, and reduced bearing capa-
city compared to Mast 7. A fully coupled thermo-hydro-mechanical (THM) model was developed to
simulate site-specific freeze-thaw processes and their impact on the foundations’ stability. Findings
reveal that ice-rich, fine-grained soils contribute to long-term settlement under thawing conditions.
Based on these insights, a pile foundation design is proposed and evaluated as a resilient alternative
to the existing shallow foundations. The study demonstrates the importance of integrating field
data, laboratory testing, and numerical modeling to assess and mitigate foundation performance
in permafrost terrain.

13
14
The three main risk factors in permafrost engineering are (1) the geological setting,
(2) water/ice availability, and (3) changes to the thermal regime. Specific combin-
ations of these factors can lead to a high risk of thaw settlement, frost heave, or
slope instability. Mitigating one or more factors—such as reducing water availability
through proper drainage—can significantly lower the risk of failure (Aastrup et al.
2018). . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . .

Sensitivity analysis of material input parameters. Parameters marked with a green

60
51
Assumed contributors to instabilities at power line masts in Sisimiut. . . . . . . . .
61
52
Phase 1: Initial soil conditions with active layer and permafrozen ground. High
bearing capacity due to the frozen state.
Phase 2: Thermal disturbance due to
construction.
Heating of permafrost.
Phase 3: Unfavorable load case leads to
bearing capacity failure. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . .
63
53
Phase 1: Initial soil conditions with active layer and ice lenses in permafrozen
ground. Phase 2: Thaw of ice lenses due to thermal disturbance during and after
construction. Phase 3: Extension of thermal disturbances in summer leads to the
melting of deeper ice lenses and settlements of the foundation. Phases 2 and 3 could
follow a seasonal cycle. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . .
64
54
Phase 1: Frost-susceptible, low-permeability soil in natural state. Phase 2: Excav-
ation and poor compaction lead to increased permeability and groundwater inflow.
Ice lenses form and grow in winter. Phase 3: Seasonal thawing of ice lenses causes
settlements.
Uplift and settlement occur cyclically, resulting in differential mast
movements. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . .
65
55
Decision tree for monitoring and mitigating existing and planned power lines on
permafrost terrain. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . .

==PAGE:17==
1
Introduction
Critical infrastructure in Arctic regions faces increasing risks due to extreme climate conditions
and accelerating permafrost degradation. In remote areas like Greenland, the reliable operation
of energy infrastructure depends mainly on its resilience to harsh environmental factors (Suter et
al. 2019). The interaction between engineered structures and permafrost is particularly complex,
as even small disturbances of natural soil regime can trigger ground deformations, leading to
foundation instability (Hjort et al. 2022).
These risks are especially relevant to Greenland’s growing renewable energy sector. Greenland
has substantial hydropower potential (Ahlstrøm et al. 2008), and investments in this field are
essential to support energy independence and climate goals. However, infrastructure must often
be constructed in remote, permafrost-rich regions, where access is limited and maintenance is costly.
Power lines are a linear infrastructure, typically spanning long distances across diverse subsurface
conditions. In many cases, it is not feasible to avoid ice-rich sediments or thaw-sensitive permafrost
entirely (Suter et al. 2019).
Since 2010, Nukissiorfiit, Greenland’s public utilities company, has operated a hydropower plant
near Sisimiut in West Greenland (Nukissiorfiit 2024). Shortly after commissioning, field inspections
revealed signs of foundation instabilities in several power line masts (Figure 1). Most of the affected
structures are located in zones with ice-rich permafrost, where ground instability poses serious risks
to the integrity of the power transmission system. Because failure of a single mast can disrupt the
entire line, the system is highly vulnerable (Papailiou 2021). Previous attempts to stabilize the
foundations have had limited success, highlighting the need for a more detailed understanding of
the underlying failure mechanisms.
Figure 1: Left: Heavily inclined Mast 12 in 2021, before stabilization attempt (Nukissiorfiit 2024).
Right: Similar pattern on Mast 6 in 2024, but not as pronounced an inclination as on Mast 12.
Prior research has largely focused on the thermal regime of the soil and assumes permafrost thaw as
the primary cause of mast instability (De Ville 2023). However, a more comprehensive analysis is
required to fully capture the contributing factors. In addition to thermal effects, hydrological and
mechanical interactions may contribute to the foundation performance and long-term resilience
(Hjort et al. 2022).
This project aims to identify the causes of the instability of masts near Sisimiut and to evaluate
mitigation strategies for future constructions. By combining field investigations, laboratory ana-
lyses, and fully coupled numerical modelling, the study develops an integrated methodology to
simulate and evaluate thermo-hydrological-mechanical interactions in frozen ground. This forms
the basis for assessing the feasibility and performance of mitigation strategies, including a proposed
pile foundation design specifically for site conditions in Sisimiut.
1
==PAGE:18==
1.1
Research purpose
The following research questions are central to this project:
1. What are the potential contributors to the instability of power line masts in Sisimiut?
(a) How do the local soil properties correlate with the measured settlements at mast loca-
tions?
(b) How does the construction method and presence of the mast structure affect the thermal
regime of the surrounding soil?
(c) Are the geotechnical parameter assumptions used in the original foundation design valid,
or does the evidence indicate a potential bearing capacity failure?
2. How can existing and future power lines in remote, permafrost-dominated regions be adapted
most feasibly to ensure long-term stability?
(a) Is a pile foundation a feasible alternative to improve long-term stability under permafrost
conditions?
(b) Could a revised shallow foundation design, incorporating updated geotechnical and
thermal considerations, offer better performance?
(c) What additional mitigation strategies and foundation solutions can be derived from
field data and simulations to ensure long-term performance of power lines in Arctic
permafrost terrain?
1.2
Main objectives and methodology
To identify potential key factors contributing to the instability of the power line masts in Sisimiut,
the following objectives are set (Table 3):
Firstly, general permafrost processes are investigated in a literature review, including soil frost
action, permafrost hydrology, and failure mechanisms. Subsequently, fieldwork is carried out at
a study site near the power line in September 2024 to create a ground surface and structure
monitoring system. A field report describes the data collection and processing in more detail. Soil
samples collected in that fieldwork are further processed in the permafrost laboratory of DTU
to classify the soil and determine key geotechnical parameters. A laboratory report summarizes
the procedures and results of these investigations. Finally, a fully coupled thermo-hydrological-
mechanical (THM) model is used to develop a methodology to simulate processes and interactions
potentially affecting the powerline stability. Processed data from the field and laboratory work
serve as input and model validation.
To investigate feasible adaptation strategies ensuring the long-term stability of power lines in
remote, permafrost-dominated regions, the following objectives are defined:
A literature review identifies and describes existing foundation designs for frozen grounds. The
THM model is used to simulate and compare different adaptation strategies. A preliminary design
of a pile foundation is elaborated to support the decision of Nukissiorfiit for their further procedure.
In this context, the suggested design is analyzed and compared with alternatives.

2
==PAGE:19==
1.3
Scope
This project focuses on understanding the geotechnical causes of foundation instability observed
in power line masts in Sisimiut. The study particularly focuses on the comparison between Mast
7, which has shown minimal signs of deformation, and Mast 9, which has experienced significant
displacement. No retrofitting has been carried out at these locations, so the foundations remain
as originally constructed. By investigating these two cases in detail, the project aims to identify
potential key geotechnical factors that might contribute to the different performance of the mast
foundations.
While the field and laboratory investigations provide a basis for characterizing local ground condi-
tions, the analytical focus is on THM processes that govern the stability of the foundations under
changing environmental loads. The Thm analysis focuses on the period between construction and
the present. Future forecasts are excluded from the analysis, but can easily be implemented in
the next steps. Structural aspects, including interactions with guy wires, anchor systems, and the
transmission line itself, are considered outside the scope of this study. Potential cascading effects
related to structural interdependence are not addressed.
This scoped approach reflects a clear research need, as earlier investigations have typically centered
on the thermal regime alone, without integrating coupled mechanical and hydrological effects.
Moreover, limited geotechnical data and an absence of laboratory-based mechanical testing have
previously hindered a thorough understanding of site-specific failure mechanisms.
This study
addresses these gaps by combining detailed subsurface investigations with laboratory tests and
fully coupled THM simulations.
1.4
Structure of the report
Chapter 1 provides an introduction to the research purpose and objectives.
Chapter 2 introduces the regional background, including the geological and climatic context of
Sisimiut, and details of the powerline design and construction.
Chapter 3 gives a theoretical overview of permafrost, its physical and mechanical properties, and
relevant failure mechanisms in frozen ground. An overview of potential foundations and mitigation
options in frozen ground is given.
Chapter 4 contains a detailed fieldwork report. It describes fieldwork methodologies, including
data collection techniques and data processing.
Chapter 5 contains a detailed laboratory report. It summarizes the laboratory tests and their
results.
Chapter 6 describes adaptations of the foundation design. It includes a preliminary design of a
pile foundation and a revision of the existing shallow foundations.
Chapter 7 shows the theoretical background and implementation of THM coupling in PLAXIS2D.
Chapter 8 presents the results and insights from the simulations and analyses. With elements from
the field report, the laboratory report, and the THM model, the research questions are discussed
and answered.
3
==PAGE:20==
2
Regional background
This chapter summarizes relevant background information about the study area. It presents the
geological and climatic conditions, as well as key technical details of the powerline infrastructure.
This information serves as the theoretical basis and provides essential input parameters for the
simulations and calculations conducted in this study.
2.1
Site description
Sisimiut, Greenland’s second largest city, is located on the west coast, north of the Arctic Circle.
Figure 2 shows the area of interest at both national and regional scale. The hydropower plant
lies approximately 30 km north of Sisimiut, and the associated powerline spans about 40 km.
The study area is located near the power plant at the Second Fjord, where most of the masts
experiencing settlement and instability are located.
Figure 2: Location of the area of interest. The left map shows the location on a national scale,
and the upper right map shows an overview of the power line from the power plant to Sisimiut.
The bottom right map shows the area around Kangerluarsuk Ungalleq (Second Fjord) where the
data collection happened. The orange dots indicate the masts’ location.

==PAGE:21==
2.2
Geological context
Figure 3: Area at the end of Kangerluarsuk Ungalleq. Left: View towards West. Right: View
towards East. Pictures from September 2024.
The landscape of central west Greenland is characterized by numerous fjords, typically around
25 km long, narrow, and up to 600 m deep, which terminate in U-shaped valleys (Nielsen 2010).
The area around Kangerluarsuk Ungalleq has the same characteristics as shown in Figure 3. The
valley around this fjord is on the one hand characterized by rocky mountains (mainly granite
and gneiss according to Dawes (2009)), on the other hand, filled with terraces of glaciofluvial and
quaternary marine sediments up to a height of 125 meters above current sea level, as shown in
Figure 4 (Sparrenbom et al. 2006).
According to Fredskild (1996), the expected soil types in western Greenland are arctic brown
soils, lithosols, and upland and meadow tundra soils. Furthermore, the area of interest lies in a
transition zone from continuous to discontinuous permafrost (Osterkamp and Burn 2003). A precise
soil classification is described in Section 4 and Section 5 in addition to the soil tests performed by
De Ville (2023).
The depth of bedrock in the area of interest is unknown. As this project focuses on soil mechanical
processes and processes in permafrost, more detailed rock geology is not dealt with but is referred
to Dawes (2009).
Figure 4: Deglacial upper marine limit modified from Aastrup et al. (2018). Below this elevation,
fine-grained, ice-rich marine deposits are commonly present (Aastrup et al. 2018). The area of
interest, highlighted by the red rectangle, lies near a local marine limit of approximately 125 m.
5
==PAGE:22==
2.3
Climate and meteorological data
Located above the Arctic Circle with a latitude of 66.94°N, Sisimiut has Tundra climate condi-
tions: extremely cold winters with mean temperatures below 0°C for 6-10 months of the year and
temperatures down to -30°C, followed by short summers with temperatures up to +25°C (Weather
and Climate 2024). The yearly precipitation is very low, with approximately 250 mm. However,
due to limited evaporation in cold climates, the soils are still very humid (NASA Earth Obser-
vatory 2022). The snow depth can reach a depth of up to 1,20m in the area of interest (De Ville
2023). The temperature and wind data from 1961 until 2022 at the Second Fjord collected by the
Danish Meteorological Institute (DMI) (Jensen 2023) and processed by De Ville (2023) are shown
in Figure 5 and Figure 6.
Figure 5: Left: Standard year for air temperature compared with the data from 2014. Right: Mean
annual air temperature. Data from (Jensen 2023) and processed by De Ville (2023).
Figure 6: Top left: Mean annual wind speed. Top right: Standard year for wind speeds. Bottom:
Resulting wind rose for a standard year.
Data from (Jensen 2023) and processed by De Ville
(2023).

2.4
Design and construction of the power line
The 15 MW hydropower plant of Sisimiut was built from 2006 to 2009, harnessing the discharge
from the natural lake Tasersuaq with a gross head of 80 m. The plant covers 100% of Sisimiut’s
electricity consumption, replacing previously used diesel generators (Nukissiorfiit 2024).
The 60 kV transmission line was constructed in the Summer of 2008 by Istak - an Icelandic
construction company. It consists of 82 masts, where 26 are built on sedimentary soil with partly
ice-rich permafrost deposits and 56 on bedrock or rocks on soil. Different foundations have been
used depending on the ground type, and 2 to 6 anchors stabilize the mast. Table 4 provides an
overview of all relevant information on Masts 6 to 14 lying within the area of interest.

Figure 8 shows a cross-sectional view of the LF foundation (Danish: fundament i løsmasse) used
for masts built on sedimentary soil. For construction, the active layer was first fully excavated
(approx. 1.20 m), and then frozen soil was excavated until a depth of at least 2.35 meters was
reached. Subsequently, 15 cm thick construction timber was laid out over an area of 1.20 m x 1.20
m as a base. 2 HE140A profiles, each connected to the construction timber with 4 bolts, together
with a HE300A profile, connect the mast to the base. A vertical insulation layer consisting of
20 mm polystyrene was then glued to the mast foundation column, and a horizontal insulation
layer consisting of 50 mm polystyrene was laid out at a depth of approx. 80 cm. The previously
excavated soil was used as a backfill. Thereby, the originally frozen soil was likely warmed up as
the construction was done in summer (´Istak and L´ınuh¨onnun 2008). The material properties are
shown in Table 79, Table 80 and Table 82 in Appendix Section C.

This project focuses on masts built on sedimentary soil. Masts built on other surface types are
excluded from the scope. In the summer of 2024, Nukissiorfiit reports issues from 19 of 82 masts,
which show subsidence or differential settlements. All of the masts affected by settlements are
built on sedimentary soil. Figure 9 shows examples of some of the problems mentioned.
Figure 9: Top left: The foundation of Mast 9 has already been displaced so that the normally
free bolt exerts a moment on the mast (and vice versa). Top right: Mast 12 and the remains
of the attempt to bring the foundation back to a stable state in 2022. Bottom left: Subsidence

Structural analysis of BBM masts
A structural analysis of the BBM masts investigates how much vertical and horizontal deformation
can be tolerated by the mast foundation without failure (Umrlc and Norby 2025). Figure 10 shows
a principal sketch of the mast foundation.
The foundation has a vertical upward deformation
limit of ca. 150 mm due to the risk of excessive tensile (coercive) forces on the anchors. If the
vertical displacement increases from 100 mm to 250 mm, the vertical reaction force increases from
1000 kN to 2100 kN, while horizontal forces remain relatively small. However, adequate horizontal
resistance is also necessary to prevent the foundation from rotating or breaking out. The masts
were tested for horizontal displacement (0.5 m or 1.4°), which is acceptable at 70% capacity, but
this analysis did not include simultaneous upward forces. If both rotation and vertical movement
occur, vertical loads must be reduced, though the exact combined limits depend on mast height,
spacing, and ground conditions and require further investigation (Umrlc and Norby 2025).
Figure 10: Principal sketch of the deformation of the mast foundation. Potential contributors are
horizontal or vertical displacements in anchors, the foundation, or the soil, or their combination.
Loads on foundation
Transmission lines are exposed to various climatic and structural loads (Papailiou 2021). Wind
loads act dynamically on transmission lines, producing variable and often unpredictable forces in
multiple directions. They depend on terrain roughness, support height, and span length. Ice loads,
particularly from rime ice accumulation, impose heavy vertical forces on conductors and structures
(Papailiou 2021). Combined load cases consider the interaction of wind and ice, especially during
and after icing events. Construction and maintenance loads are addressed through safety factors to
protect personnel. The design principles used in this study are based on the international standard
IEC 60826, EN 50341-3-16, DS/EN 1991-1-4:2005, and Eurocode 7 Standardization (CEN) 2007.
The meteorological load data for Sisimiut are based on the Greenlandic Preliminary Code (Danish
Standards Association 2020). Table 5 gives an overview of the load-cases for the transmission line
in Sisimiut, specifically for Masts 6-13. Calculation details and force components are shown in
Appendix Section D.

2.5
Geodetic basis
The local elevation system TAS2006B has been established by ASIAQ (2006) and serves as a
reference for hydrological and construction planning in the Tasersuaq area. Several reference points
have been established in August 2006 with an accuracy of ±0.005m (c.f. Table 40). The datum
of the reference points is expressed in WGS84 with the projection UTM22N (ASIAQ 2006). Since
the measurements were taken in August 2006, it is assumed that the WGS84(G1150) realization
was used, which is aligned to the ITRF2000 (NOAA 2004).
To compare measurements from later points in time, transformations to the local elevation system
and ITRF2000 are defined. These are presented and implemented in Section 4.1.
To convert from ellipsoidal heights (WGS84) to the local height reference system TAS2006B, the
following equation applies:
ZWGS84[m] = ZTAS2006B[m] + 27.7m

3
Theoretical background
3.1
Permafrost
Permafrost is soil that is frozen at a temperature below 0°C for a minimum of two consecutive
years. It is to be considered a four-phase medium consisting of soil particles, unfrozen and frozen
water, and air (Andersland et al. 2004). Permafrost occurs in about 25 percent of the land surface
in the Northern Hemisphere (NSIDC 2024).
Soil frost action
The main driving factors determining the ground temperature are the air temperature, geothermal
heat flow, and soil thermal properties. Effects of latent heat, thermal-dependent soil properties,
and non-symmetrical surface temperatures due to changing Albedo (e.g., vegetation, snow cover)
also significantly influence the ground temperature but are difficult to assess (Andersland et al.
2004). Figure 11 shows a typical temperature profile of a multi-year frozen ground.
The active layer is the top layer of the ground, where the soil undergoes an annual freezing-thawing
cycle and has its maximum depth at the end of the summer season. Directly below the active layer
is the permafrost, which has freezing temperatures for at least two consecutive years (Andersland
et al. 2004). Figure 11 shows typical profiles of continuous and discontinuous permafrost.
Figure 11: Left: Temperature profile (trumpet plot) in multi-year frozen ground. Middle: Profile
of a continuous permafrost. Right: Profile of a discontinuous zone. From Andersland et al. (2004).
Especially in fine-grained soils, where capillary rise and permeability are in equilibrium, the annual
freezing of the active layer is responsible for the so-called frost heave: As the freezing surface moves
downwards, the pore water freezes and increases in volume by around ten percent, causing a heave
of the upper layer. Additionally, ice lenses form normal to the direction of heat flow, as water
rises through capillary forces upwards towards the freezing surface. These processes are usually
not uniform and can cause a vertical displacement of up to 150 mm per year. As soon as the active
layer starts to melt, the soil weakens and settlements of the soil occur due to volumetric changes
resulting from phase change of water and outflow of excess pore water. The frost susceptibility
of soils describes the soil in terms of its frost-heaving and thaw-weakening behavior (Andersland
et al. 2004).
11
==PAGE:28==
Freezing thawing transition
The pore water starts to freeze as soon as the temperature decreases to the temperature of spon-
taneous nucleation Tsn, and nucleation centers in the form of water molecules or soil particles
are available. Ice formation releases latent heat, resulting in an increase in temperature to the
initial freezing temperature Tf (Andersland et al. 2004). The cooling curve for pore water and ice
is shown in Figure 12. In addition to the temperature, water-ice phase composition varies with
particle mineral composition, the specific surface area of the soil particles, grain size distribution,
and the presence of solutes. Typically fine-grained soils such as clays retain more unfrozen water at
negative temperatures than coarse-grained soils like sand, due to their higher capillary adsorption
and higher surface area in the voids (Andersland et al. 2004).
Figure 12: Left: Cooling curve for pore water. Right: Phase composition curve for five exemplary
soils. Both figures from Andersland et al. (2004).
Physical and thermal properties
The response of soil to thermal changes is mainly influenced by the thermal parameters of the soil
(Andersland et al. 2004). However, there is a strong interaction between mechanical and thermal
behavior and parameters. Studying thermo-mechanical behavior in fine-grained soils is therefore
crucial for modern geotechnical engineering, especially in designing thermo-active geostructures
that experience temperature fluctuations throughout their service life (Azhar et al. 2024). Accord-
ing to Carslaw and Jaeger (1959), the heat flow in a solid material can be expressed as:
q = λS∆T
d
(2)
where λ is the thermal conductivity, S the surface area, ∆T the temperature difference and d
the thickness of the considered element. λ is a thermal property of the material and depends on
temperature. Materials with a high λ (e.g., Steel) transfer heat efficiently, while materials with
low thermal conductivity (e.g., Polystyrene or wood) are insulators (Carslaw and Jaeger 1959).
The coefficient of thermal expansion α describes the volumetric behavior of a material in response to
a temperature change. Decreasing air temperatures will cause the soil to contract, while increasing
temperatures will cause a volumetric expansion (Andersland et al. 2004):
α = 1

where Lo is length at some reference temperature, dL/Lo = dϵ, and ϵ is the strain. α is temperature

==PAGE:29==
depended (Andersland et al. 2004). The coefficient of thermal expansion is one obvious parameter
that shows the thermal and mechanical coupling of soil. However, there are many more parameters
and interactions: For example, soil strength, stiffness, pore pressures, and effective stresses depend
on temperature (Azhar et al. 2024).
Permafrost hydrology
Hydrological processes in permafrost systems play a fundamental role. For instance, groundwater
flow is strongly coupled with advective heat transfer that can accelerate permafrost thaw and
increase the hydraulic conductivity of the soil (Kurylyk and Walvoord 2021).
Therefore, the
following chapter provides an overview of the physical processes influencing groundwater flow in
permafrost soil, focusing on supra-permafrost groundwater as the interest of this project is mainly
in soil layers close to the surface.
Groundwater processes in permafrost environments are temperature-dependent and mostly occur in
soil layers above or below the permafrost layer (Kurylyk and Walvoord 2021) as shown in Figure 13.
An underlying assumption for many hydrological models in permafrost is that permafrost is an
impermeable layer. However, this simplification could lead to uncertainties, especially in thawing-
freezing cycles where the frozen ground and ice lenses could show flow pathways (Schulson and
Renshaw 2022).
Supra-permafrost groundwater exists in the active layer that underlies an annual freezing-thawing
cycle. In colder areas, i.e. Sisimiut, it freezes entirely in winter. If the water table remains constant,
groundwater flow deepens through the thaw season, peaking by the end of the summer. However,
in areas with substantial summer drying, a declining water table can reduce transmissivity and
limit deeper groundwater flow (Kurylyk and Walvoord 2021).
Figure 13: Cross-sectional view of a permafrost landscape indicating the different groundwater
flows (Kurylyk and Walvoord 2021).
Permafrost thaw directly influences groundwater flow conditions. A deepening of the active layer
results in increasing shallow groundwater storage and lateral groundwater flow, especially in re-
gions dominated by supra-permafrost flow. The increase in supra-permafrost groundwater flows is
accelerating the active layer thickening (Sj¨oberg et al. 2016). Another theory of Shiklomanov et al.
(2013) states that the increasing active layer thickness resulting from permafrost thaw is equally
balanced by ground subsidence, resulting in a net zero response in active layer thickness.
13
==PAGE:30==
Considering the influence of temperature on pore ice and hydraulic conductivity, hydrological
processes are highly dependent on temperature (Kurylyk and Walvoord 2021). On the other hand,
hydrological processes influence the thermal regime in the ground with groundwater flow-induced
heat advection as investigated by McKenzie and Voss (2013). Therefore, thermal and hydrological
processes in permafrost have to be considered as fully coupled processes (Kurylyk and Walvoord
2021). The physical processes and governing equations are shown in Appendix Section C.
Permafrost-related failure mechanisms
Soils in permafrost environments are subject to various mass movement processes that may com-
promise infrastructure stability. These movements are commonly categorized as flows, slides and
falls (Andersland et al. 2004). Falls are rapid and unlikely in the relatively low-relief terrain of the
project area and are therefore not discussed in detail. However, both flow and slide mechanisms
might contribute to the observed mast instabilities.
Flow summarizes all processes, where the soil moves down a slope similar to a viscous fluid with
uniformly distributed motion throughout the material. Processes included in this definition are
solifluction, skin, bimodal, and multiple retrogressive flows. Skin flows involve the shallow detach-
ment and movement of vegetation and soil over inclined surfaces, occurring on both steep and
low-angle slopes. Bimodal flows feature a steep semicircular headscarp and a low-angle tongue,
with material moving from the headscarp to the tongue, as seen in Arctic ice-cored terrain. Mul-
tiple retrogressive flows are characterized by a series of curved ridges formed by repeated headscarp
failures, stepping backward progressively (McRoberts and Morgenstern 1975).
On the other hand, slides are movements with shear strain and displacements along one or several
surfaces. Slides can be subdivided into block, rotational, and multiple retrogressive slides. Block
or translational slides involve the movement of intact masses along planar or undulatory surfaces,
often controlled by structural weaknesses. Small rotational slides, common in thawed river deposits,
exhibit curved failure surfaces similar to those seen in clay-rich temperate areas (McRoberts and
Morgenstern 1975).
Solifluction summarizes all down-slope displacements induced by freezing-
thawing actions in fine-grained soils. Compared to the other described soil movements, this process
operates slowly with a rate of a maximum of 1 meter per year (Matsuoka 2001). There are several
types of solifluction possible in the investigated area: Frost creep involves the gradual movement of
soil particles due to freezing-thawing cycles and consequent frost heave. Gelifluction results from
seasonal soil movement driven by thaw consolidation or increased water content from meltwater
or precipitation, often resulting in the deformation of plastic soil layers. The plug-like flow occurs
with two-sided freezing, where ice lenses grow at both the upper and lower sides of the active layer.
The active layer starts to slide as soon as the lower ice lenses thaw (Matsuoka 2001).
14
==PAGE:31==
3.2
Foundations in frozen grounds
Foundation design in regions experiencing seasonal frost requires careful consideration of foundation
depth, soil conditions, and protection against frost-related effects. Proper design is essential to
reduce the risks of frost heave, adfreeze uplift, and thaw settlement, which can significantly impact
structural stability and long-term performance (Andersland et al. 2004). This chapter provides an
overview of the main foundation types used in cold regions, explains the interaction between soil
and structure under seasonal freezing conditions, and presents common mitigation strategies.
Design principles in seasonally frozen ground
Effective foundation design in seasonally frozen ground must address two key challenges: resistance
to thaw settlement and resistance to frost heave. The likelihood of degradation mechanisms such
as frost heave, thaw settlement, and slope instability is governed by three primary risk factors:
(1) the geological setting, (2) water and ice availability, and (3) variations in the thermal regime
(Figure 14) (Aastrup et al. 2018). Fine-grained, frost-susceptible soils may develop segregated
ice under slow freezing, leading to significant heave. Ice-rich soils may remain stable while frozen
but are highly prone to settlement under warming conditions. Coarse soils are generally less frost
susceptible but can form ice wedges under extreme cold (Aastrup et al. 2018).
Figure 14: The three main risk factors in permafrost engineering are (1) the geological setting,
(2) water/ice availability, and (3) changes to the thermal regime. Specific combinations of these
factors can lead to a high risk of thaw settlement, frost heave, or slope instability. Mitigating one
or more factors—such as reducing water availability through proper drainage—can significantly
lower the risk of failure (Aastrup et al. 2018).
Engineering solutions, therefore, aim to reduce these risks by targeting one or more of the three
factors. Measures include ensuring adequate drainage to minimize water availability, insulating
or shading the ground to stabilize the thermal regime, or excavating ice-rich soils and replacing
them with non-frost-susceptible materials. A combination of these approaches is often required
to mitigate slope instability and foundation deformation in warming permafrost environments
(Aastrup et al. 2018).
Stabilizing the thermal regime is particularly effective against thaw settlement (Andersland et
al. 2004).
This can be achieved by reducing heat transfer and the adfreeze strength at the
soil–structure interface (Dourado et al. 2024). Specific measures include using low-conductivity
or low-adfreeze materials (e.g., wood instead of steel (Wang et al. 2022)), adding insulation layers,
or incorporating passive cooling elements such as thermosyphons to maintain sub-zero temperat-
ures in the permafrost zone (Andersland et al. 2004), (De Ville 2023).
To mitigate frost heave, common strategies involve surcharge loading and replacement of frost-
susceptible soils with frost-stable materials to decouple the structure from the active layer (Ander-
15
==PAGE:32==
sland et al. 2004). Further options include isolating the pile with a casing filled with an oil–wax
mixture or using backfill composed of soil mixed with oil and wax to reduce tangential frost thrust
(Zhang and Michalowski 2015). Thermal piles enhance anchorage by accelerating cooling at the
pile–permafrost interface, lowering the summer thaw depth and weakening the adfreeze bond in
the active zone. Self-refrigerating thermal piles, in particular, mitigate frost jacking by reducing
adfreeze forces along the upper shaft through targeted thermal regulation (Andersland et al. 2004).
These risk-reduction strategies can be classified into either active or passive foundation design
approaches (Andersland et al. 2004):
Active design strategies accept or modify the thermal conditions of the ground. They are typically
used where the foundation can be transferred to a more stable stratum or where unfavorable
materials are improved or replaced. In contrast, passive strategies preserve the existing thermal
regime to prevent degradation of the supporting soil. These include thermal insulation, ventilation
systems, or artificial ground cooling to keep the permafrost intact (Andersland et al. 2004).

Figure 15 illustrates typical foundation types in permafrost terrain, including passive systems such
as insulated footings and thermal piles, and active solutions such as deep foundations anchored in
stable substrata. In this project, the existing mast foundation corresponds to an active approach
where changes in the ground thermal regime are accepted, and a footing is placed on a stable
stratum (´Istak and L´ınuh¨onnun 2008).
Figure 15: Different types of foundations for permafrost conditions, illustrating passive and active
design approaches (Andersland et al. 2004).
Alongside these design strategies, modern infrastructure monitoring is critical. The so-called Struc-
tural Health Monitoring (SHM) (Farrar and Worden 2007) includes installing temperature sensors
and ground deformation instruments, especially at critical infrastructure such as the powerline
in Sisimiut. Monitoring enables early detection of permafrost degradation and helps verify the
performance of protective systems (Melnikov et al. 2022).
16
==PAGE:33==
Shallow foundations
Shallow foundations, particularly isolated footings and slabs, are widely used in permafrost regions
when soil conditions allow direct placement near the surface. Their practicality and low construc-
tion costs make them attractive for lightly loaded structures, provided that long-term stability
against frost-related processes is ensured (Andersland et al. 2004). In permafrost environments,
shallow foundations are typically placed on well-drained, coarse-grained materials such as gravel
or sand to reduce frost susceptibility and minimize heat conduction to the frozen ground. In many
cases, the structure is placed on a constructed footing, which provides both mechanical bearing
and thermal buffering. Additional insulation may be installed beneath or around the foundation
to reduce annual thermal variations and mitigate thaw settlement (Andersland et al. 2004).
Figure 16 shows a typical shallow footing embedded in frozen ground. As detailed in Section 2.4,
the foundations of the power line masts in Sisimiut were instead backfilled with fine-grained natural
soil, increasing susceptibility to thaw and frost-related deformation (Andersland et al. 2004).
Figure 16: Typical shallow footing on permafrost, embedded in a backfilled pit. Adapted from
Andersland et al. (2004).
According to DS 415:1998, shallow foundations must be designed to resist both frost heave in
winter and thaw settlement in summer(Dansk Standard 1998). The design must ensure sufficient
resistance in both vertical and horizontal directions and accommodate the combined actions of
structural load, frost-induced movement, and other environmental effects (Dansk Standard 1998).
ULS verification requires that the design load Vd does not exceed the design resistance Rd:
Vd ≤Rd
If horizontal loads act on the foundation (e.g., wind, line tension), the sliding limit state must be
checked (Papailiou 2021). The design must satisfy that:
Hd ≤Sd + Ed
where Hd is the horizontal design load, Sd is the shear resistance between base and soil, and
Ed is the difference between stabilizing and destabilizing earth pressures on vertical faces (Dansk
Standard 1998).
Design considerations include both mechanical and thermal aspects. The warmest soil temper-
atures generally occur just below the foundation base due to geothermal heat flux and potential
17
==PAGE:34==
insulation effect. As the strength and deformation behavior of frozen soils are highly temperature-
dependent (Azhar et al. 2024), the design must account for seasonal extremes by using conservative
assumptions (Andersland et al. 2004). While bearing capacity may be initially high due to frozen
conditions, long-term creep and potential thaw settlement, especially in ice-rich soils, pose critical
risks (S. Liu et al. 2019). The bearing resistance Rd is calculated using either drained or undrained
conditions, depending on the seasonal thermal state of the soil. For drained conditions, the general
bearing capacity equation (Terzaghi 1943):

Construction is ideally carried out in late fall to allow freezeback of disturbed soil layers and en-
sure initial stability (Andersland et al. 2004). According to DS 415 §6.2.4, frost penetration depth
and seasonal variation in water content must be accounted for in selecting foundation depth and
dimensions. In Sisimiut, the observed failure mechanisms suggest that improper backfill mater-
ial and insufficient thermal insulation may have compromised foundation performance over time
(Andersland et al. 2004) which will be discussed further in Section 8.
Table 9 gives an overview of the forces and resistances acting on a pile foundation in summer and
winter.

Based on the summer and the winter case, two main design strategies are used:
1. Strength-based design: Verify that the ultimate bearing resistance exceeds applied vertical
and horizontal loads.
2. Deformation-based design: Assess total and differential settlements under worst-case
thaw conditions, considering creep and consolidation.
Detailed calculations using the modified Terzaghi approach and numerical results are shown in
Section 6.
18
==PAGE:35==
Pile foundations
Axially loaded piles are commonly used in frozen ground due to their reliability in supporting
structures where frost-susceptible soils or thaw-sensitive layers exist. Most piles are prefabricated
and installed with minimal thermal disturbance. Their load-bearing capacity primarily comes from
the long-term adfreeze bond between the pile surface and the surrounding frozen backfill or native
soil. End bearing capacity contributes significantly only when piles are embedded into ice-free
bedrock (Andersland et al. 2004).
Pile installation in permafrost involves logistical challenges, especially with heavy equipment. Piles
are installed and then backfilled with a soil-water slurry, which freezes around the pile. Piles must
be restrained during freezeback. Otherwise, they may be displaced upward due to heaving forces
(Andersland et al. 2004).
According to Eurocode 7 Standardization (CEN) 2007, pile foundations must be dimensioned for
all relevant limit states, considering the full range of actions and environmental effects acting on
the structure. In frozen ground conditions, piles are subject to both downward and upward forces
that vary with environmental and structural factors. Figure 17 gives an overview of the forces
acting on piles.
Figure 17: Seasonal forces acting on piles. Modified from Andersland et al. (2004) and Wang et al.
(2022).
In summer, the pile is embedded in thawed soil, and the design must account for the structural
load P, the negative skin friction caused by consolidation of the active layer Pn, and the resisting
adfreeze force from the permafrost Pp(Wang et al. 2022). Anchoring is provided either by skin
friction in the unfrozen soil, adfreeze bond in permafrost, or the strength of frozen backfill in the
case of bored piles. The tangential upwards skin resistance is mainly influenced by the adfreeze
shear strength. This is the shear stress required to separate two objects that are bonded together
by ice. Tip resistance is typically neglected for pile diameters less than 150mm due to the large
displacements required to mobilize significant point resistance (Andersland et al. 2004). No settle-
ments of the piles are expected when the sum of the structural load, self weight and negative skin
friction is lower than the upwards resistance (Andersland et al. 2004):
P + Pn < Pp
In contrast, winter introduces the effect of frost heave in the active zone, and the pile is subjected to
an additional upward force. The upward forces primarily consist of the tangential heave force Pa,
which arises from the shearing interaction between the pile shaft and the surrounding frozen soil
19
==PAGE:36==
as it expands during freezing. Importantly, as demonstrated by Wang et al. (2022), the effective
heaving force after initial displacement larger than 10mm is governed by the residual adfreeze
strength, which is the post-bond-failure shear stress. Using the peak adfreeze bond instead of the
residual value may result in overly conservative designs (Zhang and Michalowski 2015). In some
cases, the structural load P can also act upwards due to wind uplift or dynamic effects from ice
shedding (Papailiou 2021) as discussed in Section 2.4. For the pile to remain stable, the upward
heave force must remain less than the total downward resistance (Andersland et al. 2004):
Pa < P + Pn + Pp
Table 9 gives an overview of the forces and resistances acting on a pile foundation in summer and
winter.

Based on the summer and the winter case, two major approaches are used in design according to
(Andersland et al. 2004):
1. Strength-based approach: Ensure that the applied shear stress τ on the pile shaft does
not exceed the site-specific adfreeze bond strength τmax. That is,
τ = P
As
≤τmax
(4)
where P is the applied axial load.
2. Settlement-based approach: Particularly important in ice-rich soils with mean annual
ground temperatures below −1◦C. The design must ensure that the long-term settlement
rate remains within serviceability limits. This is especially relevant under summer conditions,
when thawing and creep may reduce shaft resistance.
Designs typically consider winter behavior when soils are strongest and pile creep is minimal but
must also account for summer conditions where prolonged elevated temperatures near 0◦C can
degrade the adfreeze bond and increase settlement rates (Andersland et al. 2004). Section 6 shows
detailed calculations of the explained procedure.

4
Fieldwork report
The following field report describes the data collection and processing of the fieldwork at Kanger-
luarsuk Ungalleq (Second Fjord) in Sisimiut from the 9th to the 20th of September 2024.
Anton Berggreen Abrahamsen (Research Assistant, DTU Sustain), Anders Carlsen, Danielle Bridle,
Tom De Ville (PhD student, DTU Sustain), and Samuel Schuler (Master Student) formed the team
on-site with remote support from Thomas Ingeman-Nielsen (Associate Professor, DTU Sustain).
Nukissiorfiit financed the fieldwork.
The main objective of the fieldwork was to create a ground surface and structure monitoring system
to get better insight into the processes causing settlements or tilting of the masts (De Ville 2024).
This monitoring system includes 3 boreholes at Masts 6, 7, and 9 with a minimum depth of 3 meters.
Together with the boreholes of Masts 12 and 13 (drilled in April 2024) and the two boreholes close
to Mast 9 (drilled in June 2022), the investigation area could thus be extended and covers now 2.5
km of the powerline (Figure 18). Different thermistor sensors have been installed, core samples have
been collected and stored frozen. Furthermore, Electrical Resistivity Tomography (ERT), active
layer thickness (ALT) probing, surface temperature measurements, and drone surveilling have been
performed. Inclinometers have been installed at the masts’ foundations and air temperature sensors
at 2m height above surface level have been installed at the masts. Table 10 gives an overview of
the data collection from the fieldwork.
Figure 18: Overview of the area around Kangerluarsuk Ungalleq (Second Fjord) where the fieldwork
was conducted.

4.1
GNSS measurements
All measurements in this project were georeferenced using GNSS. The coordinate system applied
is WGS84 (G1150), projected to UTM Zone 22N. Vertical positions are referenced to the local
height system TAS2006B via a transformation outlined in Section 2.5.
A Septentrio Altus NR3 GNSS receiver was used for all field surveys, offering a precision of ap-
proximately 5 mm under ideal conditions (Septentrio 2024). A fixed benchmark (T4), located near
the site, was observed over a 24-hour session to verify the base station position.
Table 11 presents the coordinate differences of benchmark T4 between its original establishment in
2008 and remeasurement in 2024. The observed vertical change is approximately −0.16 m. How-
ever, the benchmark is placed on a surface boulder, which may be unstable. Additionally, strong
solar storms occurred during the 2024 measurement campaign (Earth.com 2024), potentially af-
fecting GNSS signal quality. Despite these uncertainties, the new position aligns well with previous
data and is considered reliable for further comparison.

Table 11: Comparison of coordinates and elevations of T4 benchmark from 2008 and 2024. Co-
ordinate differences: 2024 −2008. Elevation based on ellipsoidal height. See Table 42 in Appendix
for full data.
Coordinate processing and transformation
To align the GNSS measurements with the construction plan reference system, a series of geo-
detic transformations were performed following guidelines from PROJ Contributors (2024) and
IERS (2023). The construction coordinates are assumed to be in WGS84 (G1150), approximating
ITRF2000 at epoch 2008.667 (´Istak and L´ınuh¨onnun 2008).
The geodetic coordinates from the fieldwork were first converted to Cartesian coordinates using
the WGS84 ellipsoid (EPSG Geodetic Parameter Registry 2024). The ITRF2020 velocity model
was then applied to shift the position to epoch 2008.667 using station velocities from Qaqortoq,
the nearest station to Sisimiut (IERS 2023). Next, a 14-parameter Helmert transformation using
IERS parameters converted ITRF2020 (2008.667) to ITRF2000 (2008.667) (Altamimi et al. 2016).
Finally, the transformed Cartesian coordinates were converted back to geodetic coordinates and
22
==PAGE:39==
projected to UTM Zone 22N (PROJ Contributors 2024). A full description of the procedure can
be found in Appendix Section A.

Mast foundation displacement since 2008
The locations of mast foundations were measured with GNSS and compared with construction plan

of 2008.
It is important to note, however, that the measurements from 2008 come with several uncertainties.
First, the coordinate reference system used for the original construction data is not clearly defined.
Second, the exact measurement location in 2008, whether taken at the center pin of the foundation
or another part, is not specified. Third, the precision of the original measurements is unknown, and
the only temporal information available states that measurements were taken sometime between
July and October 2008 (´Istak and L´ınuh¨onnun 2008).
Because of these limitations, the displacement quantities presented in Table 13 should be inter-
preted with caution. Despite these uncertainties, the data indicate a consistent trend of vertical
displacement, with downward movement in the order of several decimeters, as well as horizontal
shifts of several centimeters at most locations.

==PAGE:40==
4.2
Drone surveilling
While most in situ monitoring methods offer precise point-based data, they provide limited spatial
coverage. High-resolution digital elevation models (DEMs) from drone imagery can help identify
terrain elevation changes over larger areas affected by permafrost dynamics (Fraser et al. 2022).
Survey and data processing
A DJI Air 2S drone was used to capture high-resolution images of a 200 m×200 m area centered
on the mast cluster (DJI 2024). Flight tracks were designed to ensure a minimum 60% image
overlap. Five ground control points marked with wooden discs and surveyed with GNSS were used
to georeference the image set.
Processing was performed using Agisoft Metashape software (Agisoft LLC 2024) following the
workflow shown in Figure 60. Outputs included orthophotos, point clouds, and DEMs around
Masts 6, 7, 8, and 9 with a resolution of 1.7 cm×1.7cm. Derivative products such as slope and aspect
maps were generated for geotechnical interpretation. Examples of these are shown in Figure 62
and Figure 61 in the Appendix.
DEM comparison 2022 with 2024
A DEM recorded by De Ville (2023) in June 2022 using the same drone and workflow enables
a comparison with the September 2024 dataset. This provides insight into both interannual and
seasonal terrain surface changes at Mast 9. A comparison of the DEMs is visualized in Figure 19,
together with the slope, aspect, and orthophoto data.
(a) Elevation change: dZ = Z2024 - Z2022
(b) Orthophoto (2024).
(c) Slope (2024).
(d) Aspect (2024).
Figure 19: Comparison of the two DEMs from June 2022 and September 2024 around Mast 9.
24
==PAGE:41==
The differenced DEMs reveal significant elevation changes within a relatively small area of approx-
imately 100m×100m, centered on the mast. A maximum subsidence of approximately −1.3 m was
observed south east of the mast, whereas uplift of up to 1.4 m occurred north west of the mast.
Around the mast itself, a more localized settlement of approximately 0.6 m was recorded.
The spatial pattern of vertical movement aligns closely with surface features visible in orthophotos.
The areas showing the greatest subsidence correspond to the location of an ATV track and surface
runoff channels, indicating a likely connection between concentrated overland flow and ground
deformation. Such patterns are consistent with documented cases where surface runoff promotes
thaw-induced settlement and thermal erosion along preferential flow paths in permafrost terrain
(Tananaev and Lotsari 2022).
Although visual patterns suggest stronger subsidence on steep, south-facing slopes, no significant
correlation was found statistically (see Figure 64, Figure 63 and Figure 65 in the Appendix).
One potential reason for this might be that both DEMs were generated from drone-based point
clouds and subsequently georeferenced. Subtraction of these raster surfaces assumes pixel-level
spatial alignment (QGIS Development Team 2024). However, small georeferencing mismatches and
horizontal soil displacement, for instance through solifluction, may result in vertical differencing
errors (Fraser et al. 2022). Additionally, the processed DEMs include vegetation and other non-
ground features, as no explicit filtering was applied. This makes the elevation data closer to a
digital surface model than a digital terrain model, adding further noise to the interpretation (Hirt
2016). Another limitation is that the 2022 DEM was acquired in June shortly after melting season,
while the 2024 dataset was collected in September (late summer). As a result, seasonal differences
in vegetation cover, moisture content, and thaw state may introduce elevation inconsistencies
unrelated to true ground movement (Tananaev and Lotsari 2022).
Despite these limitations, this drone-based survey still provides a valuable high-resolution overview
of surface changes and allows identification of large-scale trends.
4.3
Drilling operation
Another part of the fieldwork was to drill boreholes next to the foundations of Masts 6, 7, and 9,
supplementary to the existing boreholes next to Masts 12 and 13. Figure 20 shows the location
of the boreholes relative to the mast’s foundation. The aim was to get as close as possible to the
foundation, but not to hit it during drilling. This ensured that temperature measurements could
later be obtained from soil layers that were presumably influenced by the mast. Moreover, frozen
and unfrozen soil samples were collected from the refill material surrounding the foundations,
as well as from greater depths (from 2.2 m downward), where the soil remained unaffected by
construction activities.
Figure 20: Top view of the mast’s foundation (´Istak and L´ınuh¨onnun 2008). The location of the
borehole relative to the mast’s foundation is indicated with a blue circle. Units are in millimeters.
25
==PAGE:42==
Drilling was conducted using the permafrost drill kit from Kolibri Geo Services AS (2024), con-
sisting of an electrically powered drill, a modular drill tower, and interchangeable core barrels
(45–77 mm diameter). An overview of the main components can be found in Table 43. The setup
enabled controlled and energy-efficient drilling in remote terrain, with components transportable
by two persons. Power was supplied by synchronized generators, and setup time per borehole was
approximately one hour (Figure 21). The site set-up is shown in Figure 21.
Figure 21: Typical site set-up for the drilling operation. This one is at Mast 7.
Drilling began with the largest barrel, switching to smaller diameters when sample recovery be-
came difficult. Meltwater occasionally hindered sampling, but drilling continued until recoverable
material was reached.
All recovered soil samples were photographed, labeled, and preliminarily described following the
procedure by Agergaard et al. (2012). Frozen samples were stored at −3 ◦C in a portable freezer
powered by the setup described in De Ville (2023) (Figure 22). Table 14 gives an overview of
the boreholes drilled during the fieldwork. Detailed preliminary borehole logs are in Appendix
Section A.

4.4
Electrical Resistivity Tomography
Electrical Resistivity Tomography (ERT) has become a widely used method for investigating per-
mafrost due to its ability to distinguish between frozen and unfrozen ground. As a general rule, the
resistivity of soil increases at negative temperatures with increasing freezing of pore water (Hauck
and Vonder M¨uhll 2003). However, ERT surveys in permafrost environments are subject to signi-
ficant challenges in data acquisition, processing, and interpretation (Herring et al. 2023). Despite
these challenges, ERT remains a reliable tool for permafrost studies when applied carefully; this
study follows the best practice guidelines of Herring et al. (2023).
ERT profiles were collected at Masts 6, 7, and 9. One profile was 80 meters long and had 80
electrodes with 1-meter spacing. The aim was to use two profiles to create an orthogonal grid with
the borehole as close as possible to the center to be able to use information from the soil samples
when interpreting the ERT data (c.f. Figure 23). On the other side, a minimum distance of 5 m
was maintained to avoid interference of the ERT measurements with the steel foundation (Herring
et al. 2023).
Figure 23: ERT profiles per mast in relation to the borehole.
An ABEM Terrameter LS 2 was used for the measurements. Every electrode’s location has been
measured with GNSS. Additionally, active layer probing has been carried out at each electrode.
Finally, the surface characteristics, e.g., vegetation, surface run-offs, or slopes, along each ERT
have been described. The positions of the first and last electrode from every profile are marked
with a metal rod. This gives the possibility to measure the exact same profiles in the future.
The chosen measurement protocol was the Wenner protocol (Guideline Geo 2016) as this array
type ensures high signal-to-noise ratios and sufficient vertical resolution in permafrost (Herring
et al. 2023). The data from the measurements show apparent resistivity. With a mathematical
inversion, the actual resistivity as a function of depth is derived from the raw data. This inversion
was carried out with the software of Aarhus GeoSoftware (2024). Appendix Section A describes
the inversion settings in more detail.
To support correct interpretation of the ERT data, the following section briefly summarizes the
theoretical background of electrical resistivity in permafrost terrain. As previously mentioned,
electrical resistivity increases with the degree of freezing due to the reduction of unfrozen water
content in the soil (Spaans and Baker 1996). However, resistivity is also influenced by other factors,
particularly lithology and the salinity of pore water (J. Liu et al. 2020).
It is important to note that resistivity inversions are subject to non-uniqueness, meaning that
multiple equivalent models can fit the measured data equally well within uncertainty limits. Phe-
nomena such as high-low-high-resistivity layering can result in ambiguous interpretations of layer
thickness or depth. This is particularly relevant in settings where freeze–thaw cycles may cause
alternating layers of frozen and unfrozen material, such as thawed zones between refrozen layers,
27
==PAGE:44==
which can lead to misleading patterns in the resistivity profile (Christensen and Christiansen 2020).
Typical resistivity values for air, water, and ice are ∞, 101 - 102 and 106 - 108 Ωm respectively
Hauck and Kneisel (2008). Figure 24 further illustrates how electrical resistivity in various soils
and rocks evolves with decreasing temperature, emphasizing the strong resistivity contrast between
frozen and unfrozen states.
Figure 24: Published laboratory measurements of electrical resistivity as a function of temperature
for a variety of soils and rocks (Herring et al. 2023).
Figure 25 shows one inverted profile from Mast 9. Specifically for Mast 9, there are two identical
profiles from De Ville (2023) from June 2022, which are also shown for comparison. The same
inversion settings were used, but the profiles from 2022 are only 40 m long with the same electrode
spacing of 1 m. This results in lower resolution of deep layers than in the 2024 profiles (Herring
et al. 2023).
Figure 25: ERT profiles around Mast 9. The black line indicates the active layer thickness. The
profiles from September 2024 are compared with the profiles from the fieldwork in June 2022 (De
Ville 2023)
The ERT profiles shown in Figure 25 highlight several key features which are labeled in the figure
and discussed in the following:
1a, 1b, 1c: Zones of high resistivity (>10 000 Ωm) are visible in both profiles and likely correspond
to well-frozen, coarse-grained or ice-rich material. Reference values from Figure 23 support this
interpretation. In area 1c, resistivity values in 2024 are noticeably lower than in 2022, potentially
indicating seasonal variation due to increased water content or reduced ice saturation (Hauck and
28
==PAGE:45==
Vonder M¨uhll 2003).
2: Close to the mast foundation, both ERT datasets show lower resistivity values (100 Ωm to
500 Ωm), consistent with disturbed or backfilled soils. In the 2024 profile, the active layer correlates
well with resistivities below 1000 Ωm, and resistivity at the surface is slightly lower than in 2022,
which again likely reflects seasonal thaw effects (Hauck and Vonder M¨uhll 2003). The borehole
SISF-04 from De Ville (2023) confirms frozen silty clay up to a depth of 60cm.
3: Resistivity in 2022 are around 500 Ωm, while in 2024 they range from 100 Ωm to 400 Ωm. This
difference may result from the higher resolution of the 2024 survey (Kneisel et al. 2007) or seasonal
variation. In both cases, the data suggest altered or disturbed soil conditions. Borehole data from
Mast 9 support this interpretation: sample 15 (taken at 5.50 m depth) consists of silty clay with
visible ice lenses (Appendix Section A). This matches the resistivity range for silty clay observed
in Figure 24. The resistivity anomaly in this zone appears to follow surface topography downslope
away from the mast foundation.
Figure 26 shows the inverted profiles from Mast 7. Figure 26 shows the inverted ERT profiles at
Mast 7. The overall resistivity distribution near the mast follows a similar pattern to that observed
at Mast 9.
Figure 26: ERT profiles around Mast 7. The black line indicates the active layer thickness. Both
profiles are from September 2024.
1: A zone of moderately low resistivity (around 2000 Ωm) is present upslope, which may be related
to lateral drainage or increased unfrozen moisture due to the eastward slope (Spaans and Baker
1996).
2a, 2b, 2c: Tube-shaped high-resistivity features (>10 000 Ωm) extend downslope, suggesting
frozen or coarse-grained material aligned with the topography (Hauck and Vonder M¨uhll 2003).
These features are interrupted near the mast, possibly due to thermal disturbance or altered ground
conditions (Spaans and Baker 1996).
3: Below the mast foundation, a zone of lower resistivity ( 2000 Ωm) appears, similar in form to the
anomalies observed at Mast 9. In the side view, the feature appears to follow the slope downward,
indicating a possible hydrological or thermal flow path. Compared to Mast 9, the higher resistivity
in this zone could suggest coarser-grained material (see Figure 23 and the borehole log of Mast 7
shows loam from 2.80m depth in Appendix Section A).
The ERT profiles at Mast 6 show a similar pattern to those observed at the shown profiles. A
complete overview of all ERT profiles is presented in Appendix Section A.
Several limitations and potential sources of error must be considered when interpreting the ERT
results. Clay-rich materials can produce atypically low resistivity values, which may lead to misin-
terpretation of frozen vs. unfrozen zones (Herring et al. 2023). Furthermore, the ERT profiles from
2022 and 2024 were not collected at the same time of year, introducing possible seasonal effects
29
==PAGE:46==
related to moisture content and thaw depth (Wilkinson et al. 2012). Technical issues may also
have affected data quality, including faulty equipment. Additionally, external noise from nearby
power lines may have influenced measurements. Although such signals are coherent and could the-
oretically be filtered out using denoising techniques (e.g., low-pass filtering to suppress frequencies
above 16.6 Hz and background drift removal), this would require induced polarization (IP) data,
which was not collected in this study (Rossi et al. 2018).
In addition, a 400 m long ERT profile (80 electrodes at 5 m spacing) was measured between Masts 8
and 9 to increase the investigation depth (Kneisel et al. 2007) and to potentially identify the bedrock
interface. However, this attempt was unsuccessful, as the bedrock could not be clearly detected
in the profile (Figure 87 in Appendix). Beyond the mentioned limitations of ERT, the presence
of strongly consolidated marine soils and weak bedrock further complicates the interpretation and
makes reliable identification of the bedrock in this area particularly difficult (Nielsen 2010).
4.5
Active layer thickness probing
The active layer thickness (ALT) has been measured in a grid around the masts (Figure 27) and
along the ERT profiles. Therefore, a 1.2-meter-long T-shaped steel bar was manually pushed into
the soil until a hard layer was reached. This could be either frozen soil or boulders, which were
documented accordingly. In addition, the type of vegetation and surface runoff/wet spots were
noted at each measuring point. They were imported into QGIS and interpolated for visualization.
Triangulated Irregular Network (TIN) interpolation has been used for this (QGIS Development
Team 2024).
Figure 27: Local grid of the ALT probing. The mast is at the center. Each measuring point is
marked with a red cross. The gaps between measuring points increase as the distance to the mast
increases. The distances between the points are the same on each axis as shown in the legend. The
grid was laid with measuring tapes and measured with GNSS.
In addition to the ALT probing, the temperature at a depth of 20 cm at each measurement point
has been measured. For this, the Fluke 1524 Reference Thermistor (Fluke 2008) has been used.
Even though the measurements were very precise, the device was only suitable for field use to a
limited extent, as each measurement took several minutes. Nevertheless, it was possible to generate
a grid around Masts 6, 7, and 9 from near-surface ground temperatures in addition to ALT probing.
Figure 28 shows an example of the interpolated ALT and temperature map around Mast 7 and
Mast 9. At Mast 7, the active layer is generally deeper, with a shallower area observed south of the
mast. This shallow region is attributed to large rocks rather than frozen soil. No clear correlation
between active layer thickness and the measured temperature at 20 cm depth is evident. However,
elevated temperatures are found directly around the mast. The generally deeper ALT at Mast 7
is consistent with the coarser, sandier soil found at this location, as sandy soils typically develop
a thicker active layer than finer-grained silty or clayey soils (Andersland et al. 2004).
30
==PAGE:47==
At Mast 9, a more consistent pattern is observed. Both the active layer thickness and near-surface
ground temperature are higher close to the mast. In contrast, further away from the mast, the
active layer is shallower, and ground temperatures are lower. This spatial relationship aligns well
with the ERT results, which indicate increased soil disturbance below and around the mast.
Figure 28: Interpolated ALT (right) and temperature at 20cm depth (left) around Mast 7. Green
dots indicate the foundation of mast and barduns. The orange dot indicates the borehole.
Figure 29: Interpolated ALT (right) and temperature at 20cm depth (left) around Mast 9. Green
dots indicate the foundation of mast and barduns. The orange dot indicates the borehole.
4.6
Temperature measurements
In addition to the ground surface temperature measurements described in Section 4.5, different
temperature sensors have been installed to collect long-term temperature data in different soil
depths and locations.
GeoPrecision thermistor strings
GeoPrecision thermistor strings have been installed in every borehole (GeoPrecision GmbH 2024).
To protect the GeoPrecision from freezing groundwater, it was placed in a PEH Rotek tube with a
diameter of 32 mm. Furthermore, an anchor has been added to the bottom of the tube to prevent it
from frost heave (De Ville 2024). Sand has been used as a refill between the tube and the borehole.
Twelve thermistors are on one 3 m long GeoPrecision string with increasing spacing towards the
bottom, i.e. Table 15. The resolution of the TNode thermistor is 0.01 °C with a base accuracy of
± 0.1 °C (GeoPrecision GmbH 2024).

Figure 30 shows the temperature profile for Mast 7, covering the period from September 2024 to
April 2025 (processed by De Ville (2024)). The active layer depth is estimated to reach approx-
imately 2 m, where the maximum temperature approaches 0◦C. This depth corresponds to the
foundation footing and is therefore of particular interest for future monitoring due to its thermal
sensitivity. At this stage, no Mean Annual Ground Temperature (MAGT) can be determined, as
the available dataset spans only part of the annual cycle.
Subsurface temperatures vary between −12◦C and +7◦C over the observed period. At a depth of
3m, maximum recorded temperatures remain close to −1◦C, indicating that the soil is near the
freezing threshold even at this depth (Andersland et al. 2004). Long-term monitoring is required to
capture seasonal thermal dynamics and assess potential permafrost degradation trends. Appendix
Section A shows temperature curves from Mast 6 and 9 in addition.
(a) Temperature profile of Mast 7.
(b) Temperature trend of Mast 7.
Figure 30: Temperature data from Mast 7 from September 2024 to April 2025. Processed by
De Ville (2024).
TOMST
Ten TOMST sensors distributed over the study area were installed to measure air and soil temper-
atures as well as soil moisture. Their batteries and local memories have the theoretical capacity
to collect data for approximately ten years (TOMST 2024). The sensors were distributed across a
diverse range of locations near the masts, including areas with varying moisture levels (very moist
or dry), vegetation density (dense or sparse), and terrain slopes. One disadvantage is that the data
must be extracted directly from the devices using a connector, which is planned to be done in a
yearly interval.
iButtons
Lastly, twenty Thermochron iButtons (iButtonLink 2024) have been distributed around Mast 6, 7,
and 9 at a depth of 30 cm. The idea is to create a connecting line between the two Tomst sensors
and to have a surface ground temperature measurement along the slope orthogonal to the power
line. The iButtons also serve as an additional option for validating measurement data.
The sensors are wrapped in armored tape to protect them from moisture. In addition, two to three
32
==PAGE:49==
sensors were installed per measuring point to prevent possible unreliability (e.g., battery failure)
or measurement inaccuracies. One disadvantage, similar to the Tomst sensors, is that they have
to be read out manually and on-site.
Climate sensors
Climate sensors from TekBox (2024b) were attached to Mast 6, 7, 9, and 12 at a height of 2 meters
to measure air temperature, relative humidity, and barometric pressure. In addition, a Campbell
Scientific RAD 06 radiation shield was attached to each mast. The sensors are connected to the
LoRa system and follow the SDI-12 communication protocol (De Ville 2024).
4.7
Inclinometer measurements
An SDI-12 compass/inclinometer sensor from TekBox (2024a) has been installed at the foundations
of Mast 6, 7, 9, and 12. It measures heading, roll, and pitch angle and has an operating temperature
range from -40°C up to +80°C. It is important to mention here that due to technical issues, the
inclinometers on some masts were attached to the foundation, while on other masts they were
attached directly to the mast (cf. Figure 31). As the systems move independently of each other,
data cannot be compared with each other.
Figure 31: Left: Inclinometer at the foundation of Mast 9.
Right: Inclinometer at the mast
structure of Mast 7. Pictures from De Ville (2024).

5.1
Soil classification
The soil classification follows the scheme illustrated in Figure 91 (Appendix), developed by Arctic
DTU (Ingeman-Nielsen 2024a): Firstly, the samples are described based on visual description and
bulk density is measured in a frozen state. After drying at 50°C, the gravimetric water content, the
grain size distribution, the organic content, and the grain size density can be determined. Table 18
and Table 19 summarize the results of the soil classification tests for Mast 7 and Mast 9. Due to
time constraints, no Atterberg limits were determined. All samples are stored so that this work
can be continued in the future.
