Water content
Water content was determined following NS/EN ISO17892-1:2016.
Determining water content
forms the basis for estimating ice content and thaw deformation potential (ISO 2014). Samples
were first oven-dried at 50°C and then at 105°C to constant mass. Bulk density was measured
before drying using a 3D scan, and gravimetric water content was calculated relative to dry mass.
Detailed results and explanation are shown in Appendix Section B.
Particle size distribution
This test is critical for characterizing frost susceptibility and is used in the calculation of unfrozen
water saturation in the THM model (S.A. Ghoreishian Amiri et al. 2016) as explained in Section 7.
The particle size distribution was determined using a wet sieving procedure following NS/EN ISO
17892-4:2016. Soil samples were first dried at 50°C for 24 hours and then sieved through a series of
1 mm, 500 µm, 250 µm, 125 µm, and 63 µm meshes using distilled water. Particles smaller than 63
µm were flushed away. After sieving, the retained material was dried again at 50°C and weighed.
The procedure is explained in more detail in Appendix Section B.
In addition to wet sieving, a Pario Soil Particle Analyzer from METER Group (METER Group,
Inc. 2023) was used to determine the fine fraction (≤63m) of the samples. As recommended
in the manufacturer’s manual, samples were first passed through a 2mm sieve before analysis.
35
==PAGE:52==
Subsamples were suspended in distilled water with a dispersant, and sedimentation was measured
with the Pario to determine the particle size distribution in the silt and clay range. Since the
organic matter content was found to be below 1.5% (see Section 5.1), no pretreatment to remove
organic content was necessary. Subsamples were suspended in distilled water and a dispersant,
and sedimentation was measured with the Pario. This allowed precise determination of particle
size distribution in the silt and clay range. A more detailed description of this test can be found
in Appendix Section B.
Grain density
This parameter is required to calculate the void ratio, degree of water and ice saturation, and
serves as an input parameter for the Pariometer analysis. The determination of particle density
was performed in accordance with NS/EN ISO 17892-3:2015, using the pycnometer method. Oven-
dried (50°C) soil specimens were weighed and placed in Pycnometer, after which de-aired distilled
water was added as the control fluid.
The Pycnometer were placed in a vacuum chamber for
at least 24 hours to remove entrapped air. Subsequently, the mass of the pycnometer with the
soil and control fluid was recorded. The particle density was calculated based on the measured
masses, control fluid density, and pycnometer volume. Detailed calculations are shown in Appendix
Section B.
Organic matter content
Organic matter content was determined using Loss on Ignition (LOI) at 550°C following DS/EN
ISO 15935:2021. All samples showed LOI values below the critical threshold of 1.5%, ensuring no
interference with sedimentation measurements. Details are shown in Appendix Section B.
5.2
Design profile for Mast 7 and Mast 9
Based on the findings of the soil classification tests, the following design soil profiles are defined
for Mast 7 and Mast 9. At Mast 7, a layer of sandy clay loam extends to a depth of approximately
2.70 m, underlain by a layer of loam. The local frost table is estimated to be at a depth of 1.50 m.
At Mast 9, the upper soil layer consists of silty clay loam down to 2.00 m, followed by a layer of
silty clay. The frost table at this location is estimated at 1.25 m depth. At both locations, the
groundwater level is estimated to be at 1.00 m depth.
(a) Design profile for Mast 7.
(b) Design profile for Mast 9.
Figure 32: Design profiles for Mast 7 and Mast 9.

==PAGE:53==
5.3
Oedometer test
A temperature-controlled consolidation test series was conducted during the laboratory work. The
test follows the guideline of Ingeman-Nielsen (2017) based on the modified DS/CEN ISO/TS 17892-
5 standard. This test is used to investigate the thermo-mechanical behaviour of soils from Mast 7
and Mast 9, to derive input parameters for the THM model, and to support hand calculations of
deformation as a function of thermal changes. A detailed description can be found in Appendix
Section B.
Sample selection and preparation
Two samples were selected for testing: one from Mast 7 and one from Mast 9 (Table 20). The
selection was based on their differing soil classification profiles, as Mast 7 is located in a more stable
zone, while Mast 9 represents a less stable soil condition. In general, care was taken to ensure that
the samples did not have visible ice lenses. To assess the reproducibility and consistency of the
testing method, two subsamples were prepared from the same sample. Due to the duration of each
test iteration, approximately 40 days, only two full test iterations could be completed within the
available time frame.

Table 20: Overview of samples selected for the oedometer testing. Detailed properties are provided
in Appendix Section B.
For the temperature-controlled consolidation tests, the frozen samples were extracted in a cold-
room environment to maintain frozen conditions throughout preparation.
Each specimen was
shaped to fit into the oedometer ring, with particular attention paid to parallel end surfaces and
proper contact with the ring walls. Excess material from the trimming process was collected and
stored for classification testing.
Test method
All tests were carried out using a conventional oedometer system placed within a temperature-
controlled chamber. A detailed sketch of the experimental setup is shown in Figure 33.
Figure 33: Sketch of the oedometer cell (Ingeman-Nielsen 2017). 1) Specimen, 2) specimen ring, 3)
constraining steel ring, 4) top and bottom filter stone, 5) steel base, 6) PT-100 sensor, 7) pressure
head and 8) LVDT sensor.
37
==PAGE:54==
The system is instrumented with PT100 temperature sensors for continuous monitoring of specimen
temperature and LVDT displacement sensors to measure vertical deformation during each load and
temperature step. Data acquisition was handled by a Campbell Scientific CDM-A116 datalogger
(Ingeman-Nielsen 2017). Before the test, the LVDT sensors were calibrated (see Figure 99 and
Table 68 in Appendix).
The test procedure follows a predefined load and temperature program, consisting of 28 consecutive
steps. Figure 34 shows a potential outcome of the test. The test begins with mechanical loading of
the frozen specimen at -10°C by increasing the vertical stress from 1.5 kPa to 600 kPa (A). This is
followed by a thawing phase at constant load, during which the temperature is gradually increased
to 20°C (B). After thawing, the sample is unloaded (C) and reloaded (D) at room temperature.
To determine the final specimen height, the sample is finally unloaded again (E). The full program
is shown in Appendix Section B. During the steps C to E, distilled water is added to ensure full
saturation (Ingeman-Nielsen 2017). This is possible because the salinity of the soil in the area of
interest is assumed to be negligibly low (De Ville 2023).
Figure 34: Stress-strain space of a temperature-controlled oedometer test. A: Initial frozen loading
branch. B: Thawing of the sample. C: Unloading and D: reloading. E: Final reloading.
Results
The main results of the oedometer tests are presented in Figure 35 as stress–strain curves for each
tested specimen. The deformation behavior of the specimens varies significantly between Mast 7
and Mast 9. The two specimens from Mast 9 show similar mechanical responses, while the two
from Mast 7 show considerable differences.
All specimens showed negligible deformation during the initial loading phase at -10°C. However,
the sample M7 12(1) shows minor strain development in this stage, which has to be investigated
further. Most samples show significant strain developed during the thawing phase. Specifically,
Mast 7 12(1) experienced approximately 5% total strain as the temperature increased from −10 ◦C
to 5 ◦C, with 1.5 % strain occurring between −10 ◦C and −2 ◦C, and 3 % strain occurring between
−2 ◦C and 0.5 ◦C.
For Mast 9 10A(1), a total strain of 12 % was recorded from −10 ◦C to 0.5 ◦C. Most of this strain
(12 %) occurred between −2 ◦C and 0 ◦C, with minimal strain (0.1 %) between −10 ◦C and −2 ◦C.
Unloading and reloading were successfully performed for Mast 7 12(1), whereas the test for Mast
7 12(2) most likely failed due to a stuck pressure head, resulting in almost no measurable strains.
A similar issue occurred in Mast 9 during the thawed phase, possibly due to a damaged oedometer
ring.

5.4
Thermal properties
Thermal conductivity measurements were performed using the C-Therm TCI Analyzer (C-Therm
Technologies Ltd. 2015). Before testing, the instrument was calibrated with Pyroceram at room
temperature. Selected samples were conditioned at −10◦C in a climate chamber. Bulk densities
from 3D scans were used as software input. A thin layer of thermal contact agent was applied to
ensure good sensor contact. Measurements were conducted in Ceramic mode, except for sample
M9 15A, which was measured in Polymer mode.
The key reported parameters include the thermal conductivity λ, which is calculated by comparing
the sensor response to the factory calibration (C-Therm Technologies Ltd. 2015). The heat capacity
Cp is calculated from the measured effusivity and thermal conductivity, and input density value.
The density must be entered to calculate this value, according to the equation: Cp =
e2
k·ρ. A
detailed description of the procedure is shown in Appendix Section B.
Sample
Depth [m]

Table 22: Summary of thermal Conductivity and Heat Capacity at -10°C.
The results show a clear contrast between Mast 7 and Mast 9 samples. Mast 7 samples show higher
thermal conductivity values (2.02-2.28 W/mK), indicating higher ice content and more efficient
heat transfer. In contrast, Mast 9 samples show lower conductivity (0.86-1.37 W/mK) and higher
apparent heat capacity (Cp = 1050-1150 J/kgK).
These differences are attributed to the grain size and resulting pore structure (Penner 1970): Mast
7 samples can be classified as coarse-grained soil (Loam), leading to more complete freezing and
higher thermal conductivity (Penner 1970). Mast 9, on the other hand, consists of fine-grained
soil (Silty clay), which retains more unfrozen water at −10◦C, resulting in reduced conductivity
and increased heat capacity (Overduin and Kane 2006). The low conductivity value observed in
sample M9 15A is likely an outlier, possibly due to poor contact between the sample and the sensor
during measurement.
It should also be noted that these are bulk thermal parameters, representing the combined behavior
of the four-phase frozen soil system (solids, ice, water, and air). This is distinct from the intrinsic
thermal properties of the soil grains alone (Penner 1970).
40

6
Foundation design
The following chapter provides preliminary designs of different adaptation possibilities for the
foundation. The drafts contain technical calculations, drawings, and economic and logistical as-
pects to serve Nukissiorfiit as decision support. In addition, the proposed designs are implemented
as different scenarios in the THM model to analyze and compare their resilience to expected en-
vironmental conditions.
6.1
Pile foundation
As discussed in Section 3.2, pile foundations are a commonly adopted solution in frost-susceptible
soils due to their ability to transfer loads beyond the active frost zone. This design approach also
enables the retrofitting or modification of existing foundations. Figure 36 presents a conceptual
sketch of the proposed pile foundation design for the existing masts built on permafrost in Sisimiut.
The layout of the pile foundation, including the number and spacing of piles, was determined based
on the optimization of load equilibrium, as explained later in Section 6.1. Four piles were selected to
ensure a stable and symmetric transfer of axial and lateral loads, while fitting around the existing
foundation. The spacing has to be quantified in the detailed design, ensuring that interaction
effects between adjacent piles are minimized and accommodating the retrofitting geometry.
The general installation procedure involves the following key steps: First, vertical shafts are pre-
drilled to the required embedment depth. Piles are then inserted and the shaft is backfilled with
a suitable slurry, which is concrete in most cases (Andersland et al. 2004). After the piles are in
place, they are structurally connected to the existing mast footing. Finally, the old foundation
elements are cut, allowing the load to be transferred to the new pile foundation and preventing
unfavorable interactions with the old footing.
(a) Top view.
(b) Cross-sectional view.
Figure 36: Principal sketch of the suggested pile foundation.
Pile dimensions and material
Two alternative foundation solutions are investigated. The first suggests the use of steel piles. To
minimize thermal disturbance to the surrounding permafrost, the connection between the mast
and the piles must be thermally decoupled. This can be achieved by introducing insulation layers
at the mast-pile interface.
However, such thermal decoupling is only possible if there are no
significant tensile forces transmitted between the mast and the piles (Umrlc and Norby 2025). The
second solution considers the use of wooden piles. While timber offers a lower thermal conductivity
compared to steel, it also has a lower shear resistance (Abu Eusuf and Al Hasan 2013).
42
==PAGE:59==
For the dimensioning of the piles, the procedure described in Section 3.2 is followed. Detailed
calculations are shown in Appendix Section D.
Results are shown in Table 26 and Table 27.
The steel pile requires a smaller diameter than the wooden pile, giving an advantage regarding
drilling and installation effort. However, the steel pile requires a greater embedment depth than
the wooden pile.
In terms of structural stiffness, the steel pile offers a significantly higher bending stiffness than the
wooden alternative. This provides better resistance against lateral loads and bending moments,
which may be critical when horizontal soil movements occur.
The pile settlement analysis shows higher long-term settlements for the steel pile compared to
the wooden pile under identical loading.
After 10 years, the steel pile exceeds the allowable
displacement limit of 20 mm ((Umrlc and Norby 2025)), while the wooden pile remains well within
the tolerance. Additionally, the analysis indicates that the wooden pile can carry a higher allowable
axial load than the steel pile under the same creep criteria.
From a geotechnical perspective, the wooden pile appears more feasible. It provides lower long-term
settlements and benefits from a lower thermal conductivity, which helps to preserve the permafrost
conditions around the foundation. Structurally, however, the steel pile presents advantages, espe-
cially where significant shear forces or bending moments are expected. Its higher stiffness makes
it more robust in scenarios with complex loading, provided that the long-term settlements can be
limited through design adjustments.

Logistics
This seasonal constraint, combined with the remote location, makes the logistics of construction
particularly complex. An additional consideration is whether the powerline can remain operational
during the drilling and construction phases. This must be clarified before project execution. Over-
all, the logistical challenges of transporting heavy machinery, operating in extreme conditions, and
coordinating with existing infrastructure significantly increase the complexity of the project.
A suitable drilling machine for foundation work, such as the SmartROC T40 top-hammer surface
drill rig, is recommended due to the expected drilling depth of ≈15m. However, with an oper-
ational weight of ≈15 tons, the equipment exceeds the capacity for air transport via helicopter
and must instead be shipped to the quay of the powerline (Epiroc 2024). Since the fjord and
sea are typically frozen between November and June (Danish Meteorological Institute 2025), all
43
==PAGE:60==
transport activities must be scheduled during the summer period. Another challenge is that all
masts requiring retrofitting are located on the south shore, which is difficult to access due to steep
terrain and a shallow fjord. Even if the machine is brought to the dock, crossing the river and
transporting such a heavy rig through the challenging landscape would require extensive use of
special transport equipment. As an alternative, modular or dismountable drilling equipment that
can be transported in parts by helicopter may offer a more feasible solution. This would enable
flexible site access without relying on heavy ground transport.
Another important aspect is the seasonal timing for pile installation. Ideally, pile construction
should be scheduled for the winter months, when the ground is frozen and stable. This must be
considered early when coordinating logistics and equipment mobilization. Additionally, it remains
to be clarified whether the power line can remain operational during construction.
Overall, the remote location, seasonal accessibility, terrain challenges, and coordination with ex-
isting infrastructure significantly increase the complexity of the project.
6.2
Shallow foundation
Reevaluation of existing foundations
The design soil parameters for Mast 7 and Mast 9 were originally based on boreholes with a
maximum investigation depth of only 0.5m (EFLA R˚adgivende Ingeniører 2009). However, since
the actual construction extends to depths of 2.2m, samples from these depths were analyzed for
the revised evaluation. The comparison in Table 28 shows that the original design classifications
assumed significantly coarser-grained materials than those actually encountered. Specifically, the
design types correspond to silty and well-graded sands, whereas the revised classification indicates
finer-grained soils such as sandy clay loam and silty clay.
The friction angle used in the design for Mast 7 remains reasonable and consistent with the revised
soil type, but the value assigned to Mast 9 (38°) is most likely an overestimation, with the revised
analysis suggesting a much lower angle of 15°, typical for frozen fine silty clays (Andersland et al.
2004). In addition, the water content in the revised profiles is notably higher for both locations,
exceeding 20% and 34%.

Table 29: Comparison of bearing capacity for Mast 7 and Mast 9 with revised input parameters.
The bearing capacity is calculated based on DS415:1998 (Dansk Standard 1998). Details of the
calculations are shown in Appendix Section D.
The bearing capacity results for Mast 7 and Mast 9 reveal clear differences between the original
design assumptions and the revised input parameters. For Mast 7, the difference in bearing capacity
is minor (844 kPa vs. 784 kPa), and the utilization remains well below critical. This indicates
that the design assumptions for the soil parameters were reasonably consistent with the actual
subsurface conditions.
In contrast, Mast 9 shows a significant deviation. The original bearing capacity of 1151 kPa is
drastically reduced to 162 kPa in the revised calculation, resulting in a utilization of over 300%.
This clearly indicates a bearing capacity failure under the given load scenario.
The failure is
primarily due to the design assumption of a coarse-grained soil with a high friction angle (38°),
whereas the revised classification indicates a fine-grained, frozen silty clay with a much lower
friction angle (15°).
Two calculation methods were used to verify the bearing capacity: the DS 415:1998 standard, which
was used for the original design, and the modified Terzaghi approach described in Andersland et al.
(2004). The main differences between the methods are in the computation of shape, depth, and
inclination factors, as explained in Appendix Section D. The DS standard is more suitable for
unfrozen or temperate soil conditions and generally gives lower bearing capacities. In contrast,
the modified Terzaghi method is specifically formulated as a first approximation for frozen soil
behavior, considering total stress conditions and the high influence of cohesion at low temperatures
(Andersland et al. 2004). Despite these differences, both methods consistently lead to the same
conclusion: Based on the updated soil data, the foundation at Mast 9 is found to be insufficiently
dimensioned under the original design assumptions, which were based on coarser-grained soils with
higher friction angles; in contrast, Mast 7 maintains adequate stability, as the initial assumptions
were more consistent with the actual subsurface conditions.
There are two main limitations to this analysis. First, the load combination used is based on the
foundation report (Gunnlaugsson et al. 2008), but no detailed specification of this load scenario
is available. It is used here exclusively to assess the impact of the original design assumptions
compared to the revised soil parameters. Second, the strength parameters of frozen soil are known
to vary with both temperature and loading duration (Andersland et al. 2004; Azhar et al. 2024). For
this comparison, the conservative assumption has been used that the soil is close to 0°C below the
foundation base, supported by field measurements from temperature sensors as shown in Figure 5
in Section 4.6.
Design recommendations for future foundations
For the existing foundation, meaningful improvement is difficult to achieve. Past renovation efforts,
such as those undertaken at Mast 12, did not result in a sufficiently improved outcome (Nukissior-
fiit 2024). One major challenge is to estimate the extent of thermal influence on the soil beneath
the foundation (Andersland et al. 2004). All soil that is thermally affected must ideally be re-
placed with coarse-grained, non-frost-susceptible material to ensure long-term stability according
to the recommendations described in Section 3.2. However, identifying the full depth of thermally
influenced soil is inherently difficult, especially since the renovation typically occurs during sum-
mer months, when the soil is subject to seasonal warming. This process may introduce additional
45
==PAGE:62==
heat into deeper soil layers, exacerbating thermal disturbance and limiting the effectiveness of re-
placement at the existing foundations. To avoid further thermal disturbance during construction,
the work should ideally be carried out during winter, when the ground is frozen. However, this
introduces significant logistical and technical challenges, as excavation of frozen soil is considerably
more difficult and resource-intensive (Andersland et al. 2004).
Given these constraints, the recommendations primarily apply to future foundations.
Shallow
foundations may still be feasible, provided that the entire excavation pit, including all thermally
influenced soil, is thoroughly replaced with well-compacted gravel or similarly coarse, non-frost-
susceptible material (Andersland et al. 2004).
Despite this approach, practical challenges remain. The foundation sites are remote and logistically
difficult to access.
Moreover, predicting the depth of thermal disturbance is highly uncertain
without extensive in situ thermal monitoring. Therefore, the conceptual design must incorporate
conservative assumptions about frost penetration and include thermal insulation or other passive
mitigation measures where feasible (Andersland et al. 2004).
For future masts, the conceptual design involves the use of a shallow foundation placed on a fully
excavated and backfilled layer of coarse, non-frost-susceptible material, such as well-graded gravel.
The excavation must extend beyond the anticipated frost penetration depth to ensure long-term
stability. To reduce the risk of frost jacking and thaw settlement, the design should incorporate
a horizontal layer of thermal insulation directly beneath the foundation base and possibly on the
sidewalls of the excavation.
Where feasible, geotextile separation layers should be included to
prevent fine soil migration into the gravel layer.
The use of shallow footings remains feasible,
provided that construction occurs during cold periods to minimize thermal disturbance of the
subsoil(Andersland et al. 2004).
46
==PAGE:63==
7
Thermo-hydrological-mechanical modeling
This chapter presents the setup and results of the fully coupled thermo-hydrological-mechanical
(THM) model used to simulate the soil–structure interaction at Mast 7 and Mast 9. The model
reproduces seasonal freeze–thaw cycles and evaluates the resulting settlements of the foundation.
7.1
Model theory
The Frozen and Unfrozen Soil Model in PLAXIS is an advanced constitutive model used to simulate
the mechanical behavior of soils under freezing and thawing conditions (S.A. Ghoreishian Amiri
et al. 2016). This makes it particularly suitable for assessing the thermal impacts contributing to
mast instability. Originally developed by S.A. Ghoreishian Amiri et al. (2016) and later modified by
Aukenthaler (2016), the model is implemented in PLAXIS as a user-defined material model. The
detailed theoretical background has been described in the pre-thesis and is provided in Appendix
Section C.
7.2
Model set-up
The initial version of the model was implemented during the pre-thesis phase. For this project,
several updates and refinements were incorporated into the model, as summarized in Table 30.
Step
Improvements

Table 30: Implemented updates for the THM model.
Model geometry
To project the three-dimensional problem into two dimensions, a cross-section of the terrain and
the foundation perpendicular to the transmission line is chosen. The cross-section of the mast
foundation has been implemented according to Figure 8.
As a result of initial simplifications, a plane strain model has been chosen as the initial approach,
which means that strains only occur in the xy plane and are assumed to be zero along the lon-
gitudinal axis (Tjie-Liong 2014). Even if this simplification avoids potential numerical errors, the
47
==PAGE:64==
selection of the plane strain model leads to an overestimation of the results, especially an over-
estimation of thermal ground regime (Tjie-Liong 2014). For this reason, the axisymmetric model
was implemented in the revised version.
Dimensions of 5m x 20m were selected to increase the level of detail and to optimize the computing
speed. A medium mesh with local refinements around the mast was chosen as shown in Figure 103
in the Appendix. The model geometry is shown in Figure 37.
Interfaces are included in the THM model to accurately represent the soil-structure interaction
between the mast foundation, insulation layers, and piles. These interface elements allow par-
tial slip and separation and improve mesh quality (Bentley Systems International Limited 2024).
Permeability across the interfaces was adjusted to reflect realistic drainage and flow conditions
(Appendix Table 81).
Figure 37: The geometry as implemented in PLAXIS.
Thermal and flow boundary conditions
For the thermal modeling, site-specific long-term high-resolution climate data is required (Bentley
2023). As the official DMI weather station in Sisimiut is located approximately 30 km away from
the study site, ERA5 reanalysis data was used instead (Copernicus 2022). This dataset offers
hourly global climate variables over the past decades on a 0.25◦× 0.25◦grid (Copernicus 2022).
To improve spatial relevance, the ERA5 data were downscaled using the TopoSCALE method,
which allows scaling atmospheric variables to the exact study location using a high-resolution
DEM (Copernicus 2022). The downscaling was done by De Ville (2023).
This procedure was
verified against local Mast 9 temperature measurements and showed good agreement with the
air temperature (De Ville 2023). Based on the downscaled ERA5 dataset, a standard year was
constructed to represent typical annual temperature variations at the ground surface (Figure 38).
48
==PAGE:65==
Figure 38: Standard year for air temperature and fitted harmonic oscillation curve (De Ville 2023).
The fitted function serves as the upper thermal boundary condition for the THM model.
This dataset was used to derive a harmonic oscillation of ground surface temperature (Hardy and
D’Appolonia 1946):
T(t) = MAAT −A · cos
2π(t −lag)
365

where MAAT is the mean annual air temperature, A the seasonal amplitude, and lag the phase
delay in days (Table 31). This harmonic function represents the local climate condition and serves
as the upper thermal boundary condition for the THM model. To evaluate the model’s sensitivity to
air temperature, an alternative function is applied in selected scenarios, including three consecutive
warm summers with peak values reaching 20°C.

Table 31: Parameters for harmonic T oscillation for Sisimiut
A fixed geothermal temperature gradient of 0.02 °C/m has been chosen as a lower boundary
condition according to Osterkamp and Burn (2003). Figure 39 gives an overview of the different
thermal boundary conditions for each model stage.
As discussed in Section 3.1, the availability of water poses a risk for frost heave. To account for
the most conservative conditions, the global water level is set to the surface level in all simulation
stages. The boundaries at xmin, xmax, and ymin are closed for flow throughout all stages of the
analysis. The top boundary, ymax, is set to seepage in all calculations.
49
==PAGE:66==
Figure 39: Overview of the thermal and flow boundary conditions during the different model stages.
Material properties and design soil profile
The material properties used as input for the THM analysis are summarized in Appendix Section C.
The simulations are based on the design soil profiles for Mast 7 and Mast 9 (cf. Section 5.2).
Although the original design profiles for both masts consisted of two soil layers, only the more
conservative layer was selected for the THM simulations to reduce computational cost. For Mast
7, this is sandy clay loam; for Mast 9, it is silty clay. This selection allows for a direct comparison
of fine- versus coarse-grained materials and reflects the different mechanical strength and thermal
characteristics of each soil type.
For both masts, the foundation construction is assumed identical and consists of the following
materials according to ´Istak and L´ınuh¨onnun (2008):
• Steel: S355 J2G3, hot-dip galvanised in accordance with DS/EN ISO 1461-1999, with a
minimum coating thickness of 150 µm
• Wood: Strength class K18, impregnated according to NTR Class M (DS 2122/INSTA 140)
• Polystyrene insulation
Most soil parameters were selected based on published values from S.A. Ghoreishian Amiri et al.
(2016) and Aukenthaler (2016). Where possible, soil-specific parameters and initial state variables
were derived from laboratory testing.
To ensure more realistic simulation of phase change behavior in the porous media, thermal prop-
erties of water, ice, and vapor in soil pores were implemented as temperature-dependent functions,
50
==PAGE:67==
following the methodology of Andersland et al. (2004). These temperature-dependent curves are
shown in Figure 102 in the Appendix. Constant values for thermal properties of pore water were
avoided, as they can introduce significant uncertainty during freezing–thawing transitions (Ciorescu
2023).
Although the original design profiles for both masts included two distinct soil layers, only the lower
layer was implemented in the THM model to reduce computational cost. This layer is considered
more conservative in terms of mechanical and thermal behavior. The table below summarizes the
primary differences between the modeled soils beneath the two masts.
Load combinations
Different load combinations are included in the calculations. A minimum vertical point load of 25
kN/m is applied on top of the foundation according to Section 2.4. Due to numerical issues, the
maximum load applied in the model is 250 kN/m and therefore lower than the potential maximum
load suggested in Section 2.4.

Scenarios
All scenarios listed in Table 34 have been simulated for both Mast 7 and Mast 9, except for

51
==PAGE:68==
• Scenario I (Spin-up): Establishes a stable initial thermal condition before applying loading
and construction stages.
• Scenario 0a and 0b: Test the sensitivity of the excavation phase by comparing cases with
and without excavation under a standard temperature year and low loading.
• Scenario 1: Evaluates the structural response to a significantly increased point load of
250 kN.
• Scenarios 2a and 2b: Assess the impact of a modified temperature curve that includes
four consecutive warm summers with peak air temperatures reaching up to 22°C.
• Scenario 3: Examines the effect of removing insulation under standard climatic conditions.
• Scenarios 4a and 4b: Represent a worst-case combination of high load, lack of insulation,
and a warm climate.
• Scenario 5: Investigates a mitigation strategy for Mast 9 by replacing fine-grained soil with
compacted sand as refill material.
• Scenarios 6a, 6b, and 7: Analyze the behavior of alternative foundation types (steel and
wood piles) in separate pile foundation models under different load cases.
• Scenarios 8a and 8b: Evaluate the thermal impact and long-term performance of winter
construction on permafrost stability.
• Scenario X: An extreme case using a constant air temperature of 20°C and a sustained load
of 250 kN to estimate maximum displacements and maximum ground temperatures.

Result processing
To plot the trumpet curves and active layer depth trend with time, the results have to be processed
with the Python scripts shown in Appendix Section C. Firstly, a vertical cross-section has to be
selected. Then the results from the calculations can be displayed with a resolution of the initially
chosen step size, in this case, 20 days. Finally, the results can be exported in a CSV file, which
serves as input for the Python scripts. To ensure consistency with the time in the model and the
corresponding time in reality, Table 85 in the Appendix can be used.
52
==PAGE:69==
7.3
Results
Natural soil conditions (Spin-up)
Firstly, the natural soil conditions before the construction happened are modeled. The spin-up
ensures that the following changes in temperature are caused by the construction and not because
of the initial definition of boundary conditions, and that the natural soil column has a reasonable
initial state. Figure 40 shows the temperature profiles of undisturbed soil around Mast 7 and Mast
9. As expected, a deeper active layer can be seen with coarser-grained soil around Mast 7 than
with finer-grained material at Mast 9 (Andersland et al. 2004). This observation is also consistent
with fieldwork observations Section 5.2.
(a) Trumpet plot of natural soil around Mast 7
after 3 years. The active layer is at 1.37m depth
and the permafrost layer extends from 1.37m to
20m depth.
(b) Model spin-up of Mast 7 over 1200 days.
The plots show the temperature annually and
converge to a common state, indicating a stable
initial condition.
(c) Trumpet plot of natural soil around Mast 9
after 3 years. The active layer is at 1.01m depth
and the permafrost layer extends from 1.01m to
20m depth.
(d) Model spin-up of Mast 9 over 1200 days. The
temperature profiles converge annually, confirm-
ing a stable thermal regime before construction.
Figure 40: Spin-up of the natural soil conditions before construction.
Excavation phase
To investigate the impact of construction-related thermal disturbances, an excavation phase was
implemented in the model. This phase simulates one week during which the temperature of the
excavated material increases due to exposure to warmer air. The heating effect was modeled using
horizontal thermal boundary conditions with 20cm spacing in the excavation pit, each set to a
constant temperature of 10°C. This causes a gradual warming of the surrounding soil and leads to
thawing of permafrost even below the foundation level, as shown in Figure 41.
53
==PAGE:70==
Figure 41: Thermal regime of the soil without the excavation phase considered (left). The perma-
frost level (indicated by the white line) shows no disturbance. The figure on the right side shows
the thermal regime after the excavation phase is modeled.
Results for Mast 7
Figure 42 shows the temperature trend below the foundation of Mast 7 at a depth of 2.20 m.
During the spin-up phase, seasonal fluctuations range from approximately −4◦C to −1.5◦C. Scen-
arios S8a, S8b, and S0b generate nearly identical temperature profiles, suggesting that omitting
the excavation phase replicates the thermal behavior of winter construction. The applied load in
Scenario S8 does not affect the thermal regime. In all three scenarios, the temperature remains
consistently below freezing, varying between −4◦C and −1◦C.
Scenarios S0a, S1, and S3 show elevated peak temperatures of around −0.5◦C, underscoring the
impact of the construction process during the first year. Approximately seven winter seasons are
required for peak temperatures to return to baseline values around −1.5◦C. The similarity between
Scenarios S1 and S3 further indicates that insulation has a negligible effect on the thermal regime
at Mast 7. Loading alone (S1) does not significantly alter subsurface temperatures.
Scenarios S2a, S2b, S4a, and S4b exhibit the highest temperatures overall, rising to approxim-
ately 2◦C immediately after construction and stabilizing to seasonal variations around 0◦C. This
confirms that the thermal disturbance following construction is primarily driven by elevated air
temperatures, rather than the effectiveness of the insulation, especially during the three consecutive
warm summers.
In the extreme case Scenario SX, a constant air temperature of 20◦C causes the subsurface tem-
perature to converge to 15◦C within four years.
Figure 43 shows the trend in vertical displacements beneath the foundation of Mast 7. During
the spin-up phase, only minor seasonal heave and settlement occur. Scenarios S0a, S0b, and S8a
produce nearly identical displacement patterns, consistent with ground temperatures remaining
well below freezing. This suggests that excavation and associated thermal disturbances do not
trigger significant displacements under low vertical loads of 25 kN/m. Scenarios S2a, S4a, and
S8b exhibit comparable long-term settlement trends. However, seasonal heaving is absent in these
cases, likely due to the higher loads applied in S8b and the higher ground temperatures in S2a and
S4a, which remain above freezing.
More pronounced settlements are seen in Scenarios S1, S3, S2b, and S4b, with cumulative displace-
ments reaching up to −10 cm over 15 years. These trends indicate a progressive accumulation of
settlement over time, driven by the combined effects of thermal disturbance and sustained loading.
Scenario SX results in the most extreme response, with rapid and extensive deformation. It thus
represents an upper-bound scenario of settlement development under highly elevated thermal and
mechanical boundary conditions.
54
==PAGE:71==
Figure 42: Temperature trend below the foundation of Mast 7 (2.20m depth).
Figure 43: Trend of vertical displacements below the foundation of Mast 7 (2.20m depth).
Figure 44: Temperature profile of S4 at Mast 7 at 1, 2, 5 and 10 years after construction.
55
==PAGE:72==
Results for Mast 9
Figure 45 shows the temperature trend below the foundation of Mast 9 at a depth of 2.20m.
During the spin-up phase, typical seasonal thermal fluctuations range from approximately −1.5◦C
to −4◦C. Scenarios S8a, S8b, and S0b produce nearly identical results, indicating that neglecting
the excavation phase models thermal conditions comparable to winter construction. The applied
load in S8 does not influence the thermal regime. For these cases, the temperature remains below
freezing, fluctuating between about −4◦C and −0.5◦C.
In contrast, Scenarios S0a, S1 and S3 show slightly higher temperatures than S8a, S8b, and S0b.
This highlights the thermal influence of the construction process itself in the first years. However,
loading alone (S1) does not significantly impact the thermal response.
Scenario S5 ranges from −1◦C to 0.5◦C . An unexplained outlier occurs during the summer after
3600 simulation days in S5, despite an unchanged air temperature profile.
Scenarios S4a and S4b exhibit the highest overall temperatures, which begin to decrease approxim-
ately 15 years after construction and 12 years after the exceptionally warm summer. Scenarios S2a
and S2b follow a similar trend but are slightly colder by roughly 0.05◦C, suggesting that air
temperature dominates the thermal regime regardless of insulation performance. In the extreme
case Scenario SX, the ground temperature converges to 16◦C within 5 years under a constant air
temperature of 20◦C.
Figure 46 presents the trend in vertical displacements beneath the foundation. The spin-up phase
shows minimal seasonal heave and settlement with no long-term deformation, indicating a stable
baseline. Scenarios S0a, S0b, S8a, and S8b show similar displacement patterns, consistent with
temperatures remaining below freezing. This indicates that the excavation process, along with the
resulting thermal disturbance, does not lead to increased displacements under low vertical loads
of 25 kN/m.
Substantial displacements are observed in Scenarios S1, S2b, S8b, and S3, reaching up to −25 cm
after 15 years. These cases exhibit increasing incremental settlements over time due to the com-
bination of thermal disturbance and applied vertical load.
Scenario S5 also shows significant
settlements, primarily originating from the natural soil layer beneath the replacement fill, consist-
ent with the elevated ground temperatures observed in that case. Finally, Scenario SX displays the
highest and most rapid deformation, representing the upper bound of settlement under extreme
thermal and loading conditions.
Figure 45: Temperature trend below the foundation of Mast 9 (2.20m depth).

Comparison Mast 7 vs Mast 9

S4. At S1, Mast 7 shows an active layer thickness (ALT) of 1.77m, while Mast 9 has a shallower

the ALT reaches 2.00m at Mast 7 and 1.83m at Mast 9. The mean annual ground temperature at
Mast 9 is approximately –0.7°C at 3.22m depth.

Among the simulated cases, Scenario 4b provides the best match with field observations. This

Sensitivity analysis and limitations
Although laboratory tests were conducted to determine a few parameters used as input for the
model (Section 5.5), many of these material properties remain uncertain due to natural variability
59
==PAGE:76==
and limitations in available data. To assess the robustness of the model outcomes with respect to
these uncertainties, a sensitivity analysis was performed on selected input parameters.
The sensitivity index used in this analysis is a composite scaled form that expresses the relative
change in model output resulting from a relative change in input.
SIn = (∆output/output)
(∆inputIn/inputIn)
(5)
This formulation is consistent with the conceptual framework of composite scaled sensitivities de-
scribed in Hill et al. (2000), although a finite-difference approach is used here instead of derivative-
based sensitivity.
Unlike the original formulation by Hill et al. (2000), this analysis does not
incorporate parameter weighting or regression-based derivative statistics. Furthermore, correla-
tions between input parameters have been neglected. This means that the sensitivity indices are
calculated assuming independent variation of each parameter. The sensitivity index in this study
is based solely on the mechanical response, specifically the change in vertical displacement at the
ground surface. Results are shown in Figure 50. The results show that most mechanical input
parameters have the highest sensitivity.
The majority of these parameters could be validated
through laboratory testing.
Figure 50: Sensitivity analysis of material input parameters. Parameters marked with a green
frame could be determined from laboratory tests.
Thermal conductivity (λ) and specific heat
capacity (Cs) are shown with dashed bars, as only bulk properties were measured rather than
grain-specific values.
Several limitations must be mentioned in the current THM model. First, the formation and growth
of ice lenses are not included. As a result, the model likely underestimates frost heave during
freezing and subsidence during thawing, since melting of ice lenses typically leads to substantial
settlement (SA Ghoreishian Amiri et al. 2021; Rempel 2007). The model instead focuses only on
bulk material property changes as a function of temperature (S.A. Ghoreishian Amiri et al. 2016).
Furthermore, no terrain slope is included, and air temperature is the only thermal boundary
condition considered. Additional simplifications include the exclusion of lateral ground heat flux
and solar radiation. The geometry of the foundation and subsurface layers remains idealized, and
considerable uncertainty persists in the input parameters. Nevertheless, model validation against
observed displacements and thermal ground regime shows that the simplified THM setup captures
the dominant mechanisms with acceptable accuracy.
60
==PAGE:77==
8
Results and discussion
8.1
What are the potential contributors to the instability of power line
masts in Sisimiut?
The instability of power line masts in Sisimiut results from a complex interaction of thermal, mech-
anical, hydrological, and structural processes driven by both natural and anthropogenic factors
(Figure 51). This section summarizes evidence from the previously described literature review,
field observations, laboratory testing, and numerical modelling to identify and evaluate these con-
tributors.
Figure 51: Assumed contributors to instabilities at power line masts in Sisimiut.
Soil mechanical processes are a key contributor. The heating of ice-rich, fine-grained soils can
significantly reduce bearing capacity. This can lead to bearing capacity failure, especially under
high vertical loads and is further discussed in Q1.3. The relevance of soil properties is further dis-
cussed in Q1.1. While slope stability and solifluction are conceptually relevant, terrain parameters
in the area do not meet typical thresholds for these types of failure mechanisms (McRoberts and
Morgenstern 1975).
Thermal processes, especially thermal disturbance caused by construction and the presence of
the mast structure itself, are well supported by field temperature profiles and THM model results.
These disturbances modify the active layer depth and reduce the frost table over time, increasing
the risk of thaw-related settlement. Q1.2 contains a detailed discussion of the contributing thermal
processes.
Hydrological processes may also play a critical role.
Groundwater flow can transport heat
and contribute to the degradation of permafrost. The excavation and backfilling have probably
disturbed the natural flow regime and could have led to the formation of new pathways or the
61
==PAGE:78==
retention of groundwater in the excavation area. ERT profiles suggest anomalous zones of reduced
resistivity beneath the foundation area, which may indicate ongoing subsurface flow processes.
However, this interpretation remains speculative, and additional hydrogeological investigations are
necessary to confirm the presence, direction, and thermal impact of groundwater flow.
Structural processes, including the influence of barduns, imposes significant vertical and lateral
loads on the foundation. The interaction between the mast and its foundation system may contrib-
ute to localized instability, but this mechanism is still poorly understood due to a lack of structural
response data. A cascade failure mechanism cannot be ruled out. An initial local disturbance could
trigger a chain reaction, leading to progressive instability in adjacent masts (Papailiou 2021).
Climate forcing is the overall driver of most processes. Warming trends, particularly the occur-
rence of extreme summer temperatures, accelerate permafrost thaw and increase settlement risk.
These climatic changes amplify the effects of other local processes.
In summary, the observed mast instabilities are most likely the result of an interaction between
multiple contributing factors, some of which remain poorly quantified. Continued field monitoring
is essential to improve understanding and guide future mitigation strategies.
Q1.1: How do the local soil properties correlate with the measured settlements at
mast locations?
GNSS measurements indicate a general tendency of downward movement at both mast sites, with
Mast 9 showing a greater magnitude of vertical displacement compared to Mast 7. This suggests
that settlement is occurring at both locations, though more prominently at Mast 9. Field inspec-
tions further support this trend, revealing more signs of instability and differential settlements
around Mast 9 than around Mast 7.
The displacements of the masts correlate with distinct soil characteristics: Mast 7 is founded on
coarser materials such as sandy clay loam and loam, whereas Mast 9 is built on fine-grained silty
clay loam with evidence of massive ice lenses. Although both sites exhibit similar ice saturation,
Mast 9 displays a higher total water content and void ratio, making it more susceptible to thaw-
related deformation. The oedometer test data support these findings, showing significantly larger
strains for Mast 9 under comparable thawing conditions.
Thermal and hydrological conditions also influence mast stability.
Borehole data, active layer
thickness and near-surface temperature measurements reveal a deeper active layer around Mast 7
(∼1.5 m) compared to Mast 9 (∼1.25 m), with elevated temperatures near both mast locations
due to structural influence. However, the fine-grained material at Mast 9 responds more sensitively
to thermal changes, resulting in stronger thaw settlement. These effects are further supported by
ERT data, which indicate subsurface disturbance beneath the foundations at both sites. Despite
similar structural loads and thermal conditions, the finer material at Mast 9 appears less capable
of maintaining long-term stability.
Q1.2: How does the construction method and presence of the mast structure affect
the thermal regime of the surrounding soil?
Evidence from temperature profiling and ALT probing demonstrates that the mast structures
significantly alter the thermal regime of the surrounding permafrost. Measurements consistently
show elevated T20 values and increased ALT near the masts. This pattern reflects both a physical
heat conduction from the mast footings and long-term modification of the soil’s thermal balance due
to vegetation removal and surface sealing. ERT results confirm these thermal disturbances, showing
altered resistivity profiles extending several meters around the foundation zone. The comparison of
modeled summer and winter construction scenarios further illustrates that installing foundations
during warm periods can accelerate permafrost degradation and delay re-freezing.
62
==PAGE:79==
Q1.3: Are the geotechnical parameter assumptions used in the original foundation
design valid, or does the evidence indicate a potential bearing capacity failure?
A re-evaluation of foundation stability, using observed strains and soil parameters derived from
laboratory testing, indicates that the original geotechnical assumptions may not be uniformly valid
across the site. For Mast 7, the design appears conservative enough to maintain stability under
current conditions. However, for Mast 9, the data suggest a potential bearing capacity failure or at
least critically low safety margins. The combination of high water content, fine-grained soil with
poor drainage, and significant thaw-induced strain creates a vulnerability to vertical deformation.
With increasing temperatures and extended thaw periods, the risk of loss of bearing capacity
becomes more pronounced. These findings underscore the necessity of revising design parameters
when building in ice-rich, fine-textured permafrost soils.
The following section presents different theories that could have caused the instabilities observed
on the Sisimiut powerline.
Theory 1: Bearing capacity failure
One theory for the observed mast instability is a classic bearing capacity failure driven by thermal
and mechanical changes over time. In the initial condition, the natural soil consists of a stable
permafrost profile with a shallow active layer, providing high bearing capacity due to its frozen
state. Phase 2 involves thermal disturbance during and after construction, resulting in increased
ground temperatures and a gradual weakening of the frozen soil. In Phase 3, an unfavorable load
scenario, such as ice loads or extreme wind, may lead to bearing capacity failure once the warmer
soil can no longer support the structural load.
This theory aligns with observed settlements in the range of 30–50 cm, including clear signs of
differential movement. It also explains the delayed onset of deformation, as the triggering load
scenario could have occurred at any time following construction. However, while unfavorable loads
are more likely during winter due to ice accumulation, the soil is also stronger during frozen periods.
Therefore, failure would be more likely during summer, when the soil is warmer and therefore
weaker. A minimum load of 170kN would be required for bearing capacity failure at Mast 9 when
calculating the bearing capacity under the given soil parameters and using the procedure described
in DS 415:1998.
Figure 52: Phase 1: Initial soil conditions with active layer and permafrozen ground. High bearing
capacity due to the frozen state. Phase 2: Thermal disturbance due to construction. Heating of
permafrost. Phase 3: Unfavorable load case leads to bearing capacity failure.
Theory 2: Ice lenses
Another theory is settlement induced by the melting of ice lenses. In the initial state (Phase 1), the
soil profile contains a permafrozen zone with ice lenses, especially in fine-grained layers. During
and after construction (Phase 2), thermal disturbances, such as heat transfer from the mast or loss
of vegetation, initiate thawing of near-surface ice lenses. In Phase 3, seasonal warming occurs in
deeper layers, leading to the progressive melting of deeper ice lenses and subsequent settlements
63
==PAGE:80==
of the foundation. This process may repeat annually, resulting in a cyclic degradation.
This mechanism is consistent with the observed settlement magnitudes of approximately 30–50 cm
and the differential nature of the movements. Unlike bearing capacity failure, no high structural
loads are required, and the process is particularly plausible in fine-grained, ice-rich soils such
as those found at Mast 9.
However, some limitations exist: such settlements would typically
occur earlier after construction, and relatively high sub-zero temperatures (≥0°C) beneath the
foundation would be necessary to sustain the melting of deeper ice lenses.
Figure 53: Phase 1: Initial soil conditions with active layer and ice lenses in permafrozen ground.
Phase 2: Thaw of ice lenses due to thermal disturbance during and after construction. Phase
3: Extension of thermal disturbances in summer leads to the melting of deeper ice lenses and
settlements of the foundation. Phases 2 and 3 could follow a seasonal cycle.
Theory 3: Frost heave
A third theory proposes that differential settlement is caused by post-construction formation and
subsequent melting of ice lenses, driven by hydrological changes. In the initial condition (Phase
1), the soil consists of frost-susceptible, fine-grained material with low hydraulic conductivity.
Under natural conditions, this would limit moisture movement and thus inhibit large-scale ice lens
formation (Andersland et al. 2004). In Phase 2, excavation and insufficient compaction during
construction increase the permeability of the upper soil layer. This alteration allows for the inflow
of groundwater into the backfill and surrounding soil. During winter, the presence of unfrozen
water and thermal gradients supports the formation and growth of ice lenses near the foundation.
In Phase 3, seasonal freeze-thaw cycles lead to upward movement during winter due to frost heave
from ice lens growth and freeze shear strength and downward movement during summer due to
thaw settlements. This cyclic action can produce cumulative differential settlements, even in the
absence of increased structural loads.
This theory is supported by several observations: the fine-grained and frost-susceptible nature
of the soil around Mast 9, the differential nature of the settlements, and the delayed onset of
deformation consistent with a gradual hydrological transformation of the soil. Unlike the previous
theories, this mechanism involves both uplifting and settling movements over time. However, no
long-term upward displacement trend has been observed in the GNSS data, which limits direct
confirmation.
64
==PAGE:81==
Figure 54: Phase 1: Frost-susceptible, low-permeability soil in natural state. Phase 2: Excavation
and poor compaction lead to increased permeability and groundwater inflow. Ice lenses form and
grow in winter. Phase 3: Seasonal thawing of ice lenses causes settlements. Uplift and settlement
occur cyclically, resulting in differential mast movements.
All three theories present plausible explanations for the observed settlements, but also have lim-
itations. Each aligns with certain field, laboratory or model observations, but none can be fully
proven with the available data. One or a combination of these mechanisms has likely contributed
to the instabilities of the power line masts in Sisimiut.
8.2
How can existing and future power lines in remote, permafrost-
dominated regions be adapted most feasibly to ensure long-term
stability?
Q2.1: Is a pile foundation a feasible alternative to improve long-term stability under
permafrost conditions?
Preliminary design indicates that pile foundations could offer significantly improved mechanical
stability under degrading permafrost conditions. The simulated results from the THM model con-
firm that a deep pile foundation reduces thermally induced settlements and is more resilient to
long-term ground temperature increases. Particularly, a wooden pile design shows lower creep
settlements and preserves the permafrost better due to its lower thermal conductivity. However,
implementation costs require further investigation. The current design does not account for climate
warming trends beyond current temperatures. Warmer future conditions could increase thaw set-
tlement and require deeper or more insulated piles. Furthermore, the remote location and extreme
terrain significantly complicate logistics, suggesting that modular, disassemblable equipment may
be required for installation.
Q2.2: Could a revised shallow foundation design, incorporating updated geotechnical
and thermal considerations, offer better performance in Sisimiut?
Not for the existing mast foundations.
Field evidence and modeling results confirm that the
shallow foundations currently in place show significant deformation and are highly sensitive to
thermal disturbance. For future installations, a revised shallow design could be feasible under
specific conditions: if constructed on coarse, well-drained, non-frost-susceptible soils, if horizontal
insulation is reliably implemented, and if installation is done in winter to avoid thaw weakening.
However, THM simulations suggest that shallow foundations without robust insulation or thermal
control will remain vulnerable in ice-rich, fine-grained soils.
65
==PAGE:82==
Q2.3: What additional mitigation strategies and foundation solutions can be derived
from field data and simulations to ensure long-term performance of power lines in
Arctic permafrost terrain?
Field data and model results highlight that successful adaptation of foundations in Arctic perma-
frost terrain requires both site-specific investigation and a systematic decision-making framework.
To support this, Figure 55 presents a proposed decision tree for both existing and planned power
line masts located on permafrost.
For existing infrastructure, structural health monitoring should be implemented for all masts situ-
ated on permafrost, as undetected degradation can compromise the entire transmission line (Farrar
and Worden 2007). For new construction, the first priority should be to avoid permafrost terrain
altogether, for instance by routing infrastructure over bedrock or using subsea cables where feasible.
If permafrost is unavoidable, detailed geotechnical investigations must be performed for every
mast and anchoring location, including boreholes of at least 5 m depth. These investigations should
assess soil type, bedrock depth, and terrain gradient. If bedrock is encountered within the borehole
depth or is known at the location, pile foundations are generally recommended (Andersland et al.
2004).
Where no bedrock is present, the foundation type should be selected based on soil characteristics.
Coarse-grained soils without visible ice lenses (≥1 cm) and with sufficient bearing capacity (both
frozen and unfrozen) may permit a shallow foundation solution. However, if the soil is fine-grained,
ice-rich, or lacks adequate bearing capacity, deeper pile foundations or thermosyphon-assisted
designs become necessary.
If terrain slope exceeds 5%, additional assessments for solifluction,
creep, and slope stability are essential (McRoberts and Morgenstern 1975). In such cases, flexible
foundation systems are recommended to accommodate potential deformations (Bommer et al.
2010). No definitive criterion was found in the literature regarding ice lens thickness and should
be investigated in more detail in the revised version.
Figure 55: Decision tree for monitoring and mitigating existing and planned power lines on per-
mafrost terrain.
In all cases, construction should be scheduled during the winter months to minimize thermal
disturbance to the ground.
Backfill must be coarse-grained and non-frost-susceptible, and the
bearing capacity of the undisturbed sublayer must be verified. Long-term monitoring is critical for
66
==PAGE:83==
both existing and new structures to detect progressive degradation and initiate mitigation actions
(Farrar and Worden 2007).
8.3
Limitations
Several limitations should be considered when interpreting the results of this study. First, the
available evidence base is limited. Many conclusions are drawn from point-based field data across
a large and heterogeneous terrain. This introduces uncertainty when generalizing local findings to
other mast locations (Corominas et al. 2015).
Second, each method applied in this study has inherent limitations, as discussed in their respective
sections. These include accuracy constraints in GNSS measurements, limited resolution in ERT
profiles, and uncertainties in parameter determination for the THM model beneath other.
Third, no climate change scenarios were incorporated into the THM simulations. The model was
designed primarily to reproduce the site history and evaluate what may have contributed to the
observed instabilities. Accordingly, the scope of the study was not to predict future performance,
but rather to understand the processes from construction until today and assess what could have
been done differently.
Additionally, the limited project time strongly influenced the model scope. More time than anti-
cipated was required for detailed laboratory analysis, leaving less time for model calibration and
extended scenario testing. The THM model is now well-established and ready for future applica-
tions, such as comparing different mitigation strategies or including long-term climate projections.
Recommended further steps are explained in the following chapter.
67
==PAGE:84==
9
Conclusions
9.1
Summary of results
This thesis aimed to investigate the mechanisms behind mast settlements near Sisimiut and identify
possible mitigation strategies. The main results from each chapter are summarized below.
Chapter 1 – Introduction Introduced the problem of mast instability in permafrost terrain near
Sisimiut. Defined the scope, objectives, and methodology of the thesis.
Chapter 2 – Study area and case description Described the climatic, geological, and geo-
technical context of the study site. Mast 9 was identified as a representative unstable case, and
Mast 7 as a stable reference.
Chapter 3 – Literature review Summarised relevant research on permafrost degradation, frost
heave and thaw settlement mechanisms, thermal modelling, and foundation behaviour in cold
regions. The chapter provided the theoretical background for the modelling and analysis.
Chapter 4 – Fieldwork and field data Presented the 2024 field campaign, including GNSS
measurements, borehole drilling, ERT surveys, and thermistor installation. GNSS data revealed
cumulative settlements at Mast 9, with little displacement at Mast 7. ERT and ALT probing
confirmed deep soil disturbance below the foundations.
Chapter 5 – Laboratory testing Summarized classification tests and temperature-controlled
oedometer tests. Results showed that the soils at Mast 9 are fine-grained, frost-susceptible, and
exhibit strong volumetric response during thaw. The results also provided key thermal and mech-
anical parameters for modeling.
Chapter 6 – Foundation design Re-evaluated the bearing capacity of the existing shallow
foundations using measured soil parameters. Results showed low safety margins at Mast 9. A pile
foundation was dimensioned and verified to be stable under the most conservative scenarios.
Chapter 7 – Thermo-hydrological-mechanical modelling Described the development and
calibration of a fully coupled axisymmetric THM model in PLAXIS. The model reproduced seasonal
freeze-thaw cycles and simulated thermal regime and deformation behaviour. Results aligned with
observed displacement trends and confirmed the sensitivity of the soil to thaw-related settlement.
Chapter 8 – Results and discussion This chapter evaluated the potential causes of instabil-
ity observed at Mast 9, using combined evidence from field measurements, laboratory tests, and
numerical modeling. Three main mechanisms were considered: (1) a reduction in bearing capa-
city due to thawing and loss of shear strength, (2) subsidence from the thawing of pre-existing
ice lenses, and (3) formation of new ice lenses and subsequent melting due to post-construction
changes in site hydrology. While each mechanism is supported by different sets of data, such as
GNSS displacement trends, thermal profiles, and soil classification, none of them alone fully ex-
plains the settlement pattern. Instead, the analysis suggests that a combination of these processes
is responsible. The chapter concludes that addressing permafrost degradation in infrastructure
design requires a holistic understanding of coupled thermal, hydraulic, and mechanical behavior
under changing climate and site conditions.
68
==PAGE:85==
9.2
Future work
Data collection and processing
Improved data coverage is essential to minimize spatial uncertainty and support future modeling
and design tasks. Table 37 gives an overview of recommended data and tools. Additional efforts
should focus on collecting geotechnical parameters in situ, including undrained shear strength,
cohesion, friction angle, and stiffness to a depth matching planned pile embedment. The study
area should also be extended to a regional scale, allowing for comparative analyses with sites such
as Ilulissat and Nuuk.

Table 37: Recommended data collection and processing in the future.
Foundation design
Further foundation design should include a quantitative cost–benefit and risk assessment, for ex-
ample, using a Monte Carlo simulation to support decision-making (Corominas et al. 2015). The
review of alternative foundation options should also be extended beyond the Arctic region, in ad-
dition to Canada, Russia, and Norway. Cableways in the Alps, for example, also offer tried and
tested alternatives (Bommer et al. 2010). Structural utilization should be calculated based on
material capacities. Final planning depends on the client’s chosen strategy, but additional options,
such as combined cooling piles or cable relocation, should be evaluated.
THM modelling
Future model development should include a 3D domain and integrate structural components to
simulate the full system of mast, foundation, barduns, and powerline. Model efficiency and compu-
tational cost should be optimized, particularly for slope stability and sensitivity studies. Soil model
parameters should be fitted to laboratory data using PLAXIS SoilTest, following the procedures
in Table 38, as recommended by S.A. Ghoreishian Amiri et al. (2016). Furthermore, it could be
investigated if a coupling of the Frozen and Unfrozen Soil Model of S.A. Ghoreishian Amiri et al.
(2016) with the numerical modelling of distinct ice lenses (SA Ghoreishian Amiri et al. 2021) is
feasible.

==PAGE:94==
Coordinate Transformation
BACK TO Section 4.1.
This section summarizes the transformation chain applied to the geodetic coordinates measured
during fieldwork, converting them into the coordinate system used in the construction plans, which
is most likely WGS84(G1150, ITRF2000) at Epoch August 2006 (2006.667) in a UTM22N Pro-
jection. The construction plans do not define the reference system precisely. The transformed
coordinates most likely align with WGS84 (G1150) ≈ITRF2000 (2006.667). Table 39 gives an
overview of the transformation steps, following guidelines from PROJ Contributors (2024) and
IERS (2023).
Step
Source
Target
1. Geodetic to Cartesian
ITRF2020 (2024.7)
ITRF2020 (2024.7)
2. Epoch Adjustment (Velocity model)
ITRF2020 (2024.7)
ITRF2020 (2006.667)
3. Datum Transformation (Helmert)
ITRF2020 (2006.667)
ITRF2000 (2006.667)
4. Cartesian to Geodetic
ITRF2000 (2006.667)
ITRF2000 (2006.667)
Table 39: Summary of coordinate transformation steps
1. Geodetic to Cartesian Coordinates
The measured geodetic coordinates (latitude, longitude, ellipsoidal height) in ITRF2020 at
epoch 2024.7 are converted to Cartesian coordinates (X, Y, Z) using the WGS84/ITRF2020
ellipsoid parameters (EPSG Geodetic Parameter Registry 2024):
a = 6378137.0 m,
f =
1
298.257223563
2. Epoch Adjustment (ITRF2020 velocity model)
To reference the coordinates to the construction epoch, the ITRF2020 linear velocity model
is applied (Altamimi et al. 2016). The specific velocities for the Qaqortoq station, which is
the closest to Sisimiut, are (IERS 2023):
Vx = −0.01926 m/year,
Vy = −0.00418 m/year,
Vz = 0.00948 m/year
The position at epoch 2006.667 is computed as:
X2006.667 = X2024.7 −Vx · ∆t, Y2006.667 = Y2024.7 −Vy · ∆t, Z2006.667 = Z2024.7 −Vz · ∆t
where ∆t = 2024.7 −2006.667 (IERS 2023).
3. Datum Transformation (ITRF2020 to ITRF2000, Helmert Transformation)
A 14-parameter Helmert transformation is applied using IERS-published parameters (refer-
ence epoch 2015.0) to shift from ITRF2020 to ITRF2000 at epoch 2006.667 (Altamimi et al.
2016). Parameters include according to EPSG Geodetic Parameter Registry (2024):
• Translations: Tx = −0.2 mm, Ty = 0.8 mm, Tz = −34.2 mm
• Scale: 2.25 ppb
• Rotations: 0 mas (set to zero)
• Rates: dTx = 0.1 mm/year, dTy = 0.0 mm/year, dTz = −1.7 mm/year, dS = 0.11 ppb/year
The transformation formula is:
X2000 = (1 + D)RX2020 + T
where D = scale factor, R = rotation matrix (identity here), and T = translation vector.
4. Cartesian to Geodetic Conversion
The transformed cartesian coordinates are converted back to geodetic coordinates in ITRF2000
at epoch 2006.667 (PROJ Contributors 2024).
78
==PAGE:95==
The following Python script is used for the described transformations. Calculations have been
verified with PROJ (PROJ Contributors 2024).
import
numpy as np
from
pyproj
import
Transformer , CRS
import
math
# Input
geodetic
coordinates (ITRF2020 , Epoch
2024.7)
lat_dms = (67, 6, 33.16181)
lon_dms = (-53, 22, 22.67071)
h_ell = 43.441
# Convert
DMS to decimal
degrees
def
dms_to_dd(d, m, s):
sign = -1 if d < 0 else 1
return
sign * (abs(d) + m / 60 + s / 3600)
lat_dd = dms_to_dd (* lat_dms)
lon_dd = dms_to_dd (* lon_dms)
# Convert to radians
lat_rad = math.radians(lat_dd)
lon_rad = math.radians(lon_dd)
# WGS84
ellipsoid
parameters (same as ITRF2020)
a = 6378137.0
f = 1 / 298.257223563
e2 = 2 * f - f**2
N = a / math.sqrt (1 - e2 * math.sin(lat_rad)**2)
# Step 0: Geodetic to ECEF (ITRF2020 @ 2024.7)
X_2020 = (N + h_ell) * math.cos(lat_rad) * math.cos(lon_rad)
Y_2020 = (N + h_ell) * math.cos(lat_rad) * math.sin(lon_rad)
Z_2020 = ((1 - e2) * N + h_ell) * math.sin(lat_rad)
# Step 1: Apply
velocity
model (Qaqortoq)
Vx =
-0.01926
# m/year
Vy =
-0.00418
# m/year
Vz = 0.00948
# m/year
dt = 2024.7 - 2006.667
X_2020_2006 = X_2020 - Vx * dt
Y_2020_2006 = Y_2020 - Vy * dt
Z_2020_2006 = Z_2020 - Vz * dt
# Step 2: Helmert
transform (ITRF2020
ITRF2000)
Tx , Ty , Tz , D =
-0.2, 0.8,
-34.2, 2.25
dTx , dTy , dTz , dD = 0.1, 0.0,
-1.7, 0.11
dt_helmert = 2006.667 - 2015.0
Tx += dTx * dt_helmert
Ty += dTy * dt_helmert
Tz += dTz * dt_helmert
D += dD * dt_helmert
Tx , Ty , Tz = Tx/1000 , Ty/1000 , Tz /1000
D = 1 + D * 1e-9
rotation_matrix = np.identity (3)
XYZ_2020_2006 = np.array ([ X_2020_2006 , Y_2020_2006 , Z_2020_2006 ])
translation_vector = np.array ([Tx , Ty , Tz])
XYZ_2000_2006 = D * rotation_matrix @ XYZ_2020_2006 + translation_vector
# Step 3: Convert
back to geodetic
ecef_to_geod = Transformer.from_crs(CRS.from_epsg (4978) , CRS.from_epsg
79
==PAGE:96==
(4326) , always_xy=True)
lon_out , lat_out , h_out = ecef_to_geod .transform (* XYZ_2000_2006 )
# UTM Zone 22N projection
to_utm = Transformer.from_crs(CRS.from_epsg (4978) , CRS.from_epsg (32622) ,
always_xy=True)
easting , northing , h_utm = to_utm.transform (* XYZ_2000_2006 )
# Final
results
print("Geodetic
coordinates (ITRF2000 @ 2006.67):")
print(f"Latitude: {lat_out :.8f}
")
print(f"Longitude: {lon_out :.8f}
")
print(f"Ellipsoidal
height: {h_out :.3f} m")
print("\nProjected
coordinates (UTM Zone 22N):")
print(f"Easting: {easting :.3f} m")
print(f"Northing: {northing :.3f} m")
print(f"Ellipsoidal
height: {h_utm :.3f} m")

BACK TO Section 4.3.
Electrical Resistivity Tomography
The following describes the processing of the ERT data in detail. Aarhus workbench software
was used, and data was processed following the ERT IP manual from Seequent (2024). Inversion
parameters were chosen according to Herring et al. (2023).
Data Import
Appendix RES2DINV was chosen for input type. The topography was derived from the EWP-file
with the structure as shown in Figure 78.
Figure 78: Required format of the EWP-file.
Data Processing
The data is visualized as a pseudo-section, where apparent resistivity is displayed for all electrode
configurations, and as a data profile showing measurements by focus depth. These visualizations
help identify outliers, such as those caused by bad electrodes or noise (Seequent 2024). An example
is shown in Figure 79. Following initial data cleaning, a DC inversion is run. A 2D resistivity
inversion is used, starting with default settings and a smooth model. During the inversion setup,
the vertical discretization was updated.
The inverted resistivity model and the corresponding
residual pseudo-section are displayed. Induced Polarization (IP) data were not available for this
study. Therefore, an automated filtering routine for DC data, IP decay information could not be
developed as recommended by Herring et al. (2023).
97
==PAGE:114==
Figure 79: Removal of outliers.

Frozen Core classification
BACK TO Section 5.1
Figure 91: Scheme for soil classification conducted by Arctic DTU (Ingeman-Nielsen 2024a).
Lab sheet for classification of frozen cores that have to be thawed and dried at 50°C in order to
conduct consistency limit experiments and estimate bulk sample properties.
Water content was determined following CEN ISO/TS 17892-1:2004. Intact samples were first
measured for bulk volume using a 3D scan to calculate the bulk density. Subsequently, the samples
were oven-dried at 50°C to remove free water, followed by drying at 105°C until constant mass was
reached, as specified by the standard. The gravimetric water content w was calculated as:
w = mw
md
where mw is the mass of water (mass loss during drying) and md is the dry mass after 105°C
drying.

==PAGE:138==
Particle size distribution
BACK TO Section 5.1 A combination of wet sieving (for particles ¿ 63 µm) and Pariometer analysis
(for particles ¡ 63 µm) was chosen to determine the particle size distribution.
Wet Sieving Procedure
The particle size distribution of the fine-grained material was determined using the wet sieving
method in accordance with ISO 17892-4. The procedure followed these main steps:
• Drying: The soil samples were first dried in a laboratory oven at 50°C for at least 24 hours
to remove natural moisture content and to ensure consistency in mass measurements.
• Preparation: The dried soil samples were weighed and gently disaggregated using a pestle
to avoid breaking individual grains and to avoid mechanical alteration of the particles.
• Sieve sizes: The following sieve series was used: 1 mm, 500 µm, 250 µm, 125 µm, and 63
µm. The material passing 63 µm was flushed away.
• Wet sieving procedure: Wet sieving was carried out using distilled water to ensure no
contamination. The sample was placed on the top sieve, and water was used to help separate
the fine particles.
• Drying after sieving: After wet sieving, the retained material on each sieve was carefully
transferred to containers and dried again in the oven at 50°C until constant mass was reached.
• Mass determination: The mass retained on each sieve was measured using a precision
balance, and the percentage retained and cumulative passing were calculated.
The procedure was designed as a non-destructive test, meaning that the sieved and dried material
can be preserved and used for subsequent geotechnical analyses if needed.
Pariometer Analysis BACK TO Section 5.1 The particle size distribution of the fine fractions
was determined using the Pario Soil Particle Analyzer (METER Group, Inc. 2023).
1. Sample preparation: Oven-dried soil samples (50°C, 24 h) were placed in plastic bottles
and filled approximately halfway with distilled water and dispersion salt (Na4P2O7 solution).
The samples were then placed on a shaking table for at least 24 hours to ensure proper
dispersion.
2. Procedure: The measurement followed the Pario manual. The dispersed suspension was
transferred to the Pario sedimentation cylinder, filled to the 1 L mark with distilled water.
The measurement was run in Pario Classic Mode (∼8 h). Wet sieving results (1 mm, 500 µm,
250 µm, 125 µm, 63 µm) and grain density were used as input in the Pario Control software.
3. Limitations: Organic matter contents >1.5% can affect sedimentation measurements. Loss-
on-ignition (LOI) tests confirmed that the organic matter content in the analyzed samples
was below this threshold.
4. Output: The Pario software automatically calculated the particle size distribution curve
based on the sedimentation principle and the pressure-time relationship, corrected for disper-
sion salt, temperature, and sand fractions. Results were exported as particle size distributions
and soil texture classifications.
